(33) Compromise (sulh) for an unknown thing in exchange for a known thing is permissible. Translation: Compromise for an unknown in exchange for a known is permissible. 
Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that if the object of compromise (musalah anhu), i.e., the thing for which the compromise is made, is unknown, and the compromise payment (musalah bihi) is known, then it is permissible. For example, someone claims an unspecified share in a house, and the occupant makes a compromise with a known amount of money, then it is permissible. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if someone claims an entire house, and the defendant makes a compromise with the claimant for one hundred dirhams, and later another rightful owner appears for a portion of the house, then the defendant will recover from the claimant the amount of the compromise proportionally. For example, if a twenty percent share's rightful owner appears, then the occupant will take back from the claimant twenty dirhams. Whatever share is proven, that much amount will be recovered. Thus it is known that compromise for an unknown thing in exchange for a known consideration is valid. The claimant received one hundred dirhams and dropped his right, and things that are of the category of relinquishment (isqat), ignorance in them does not lead to dispute (niza'). Therefore, compromise for a known thing in exchange for it is valid. Remember that for the validity of compromise, the correctness of the claim is not a condition or necessary, because here the claimant's claim is not based on ignorance of the right, yet despite that, compromise upon it is correct and valid. Al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Istihqaq, vol. 3 p. 93: And the case indicates that compromise for an unknown thing in exchange for a known thing is permissible, because ignorance in what is being relinquished does not lead to dispute. And the case also indicates that the validity of the claim...
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents a principle from Al-Hidayah: 'Compromise (sulh) for an unknown thing in exchange for a known thing is permissible.' The Urdu tashreeh explains: if a person claims an unspecified share in a house, and the occupant makes a compromise with the claimant by paying a fixed amount of money, it is valid. For example, if someone claims the entire house, and the defendant settles with him for 100 dirhams, and later another rightful owner appears for a portion of the house, the defendant can reclaim from the claimant a proportional part of the settlement amount. The reason is that the unknown thing (the unspecified share) is being relinquished (isqat), and uncertainty in matters of relinquishment does not lead to dispute (niza'). This is because the purpose of compromise is to end conflict, and such uncertainty does not frustrate that purpose. The passage also implies that the validity of the compromise does not depend on the correctness of the original claim—even if the claim is based on ignorance of the right, the compromise is still valid. The underlying principle: whenever the subject of the compromise is something that falls under the category of isqat (relinquishment of rights), ignorance about it does not invalidate the contract.

Key Terms
الصلح (al-sulh): A compromise or amicable settlement between disputing parties. In this context, it is a contract where one party relinquishes a claim in exchange for compensation.
المجهول (al-majhul): An unknown or unspecified thing. In the context of sulh, it refers to the right or claim being relinquished that is not precisely defined.
الجهالة (al-jahala): Ignorance or uncertainty regarding the specifics of a thing. In this context, it refers to the lack of knowledge about the exact nature or extent of the right being relinquished.
الإسقاط (al-isqat): Relinquishment or dropping of a right or claim. In this passage, the subject of the compromise (sulh) is considered an isqat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a compromise (sulh) for an unknown thing in exchange for a known thing valid?
A) Because the unknown thing becomes known later by operation of law.
B) Because ignorance in matters of relinquishment (isqat) does not lead to dispute.
C) Because the compromise is essentially a sale that transfers ownership.
D) Because the claimant's claim is always presumed correct.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a compromise is valid even if the right being relinquished is unknown, because it is categorized as isqat where uncertainty is tolerated.

Sulh over Unknown for Known is Permissible: The Principle of Isqat and Jahalah
Establishes permissibility of sulh over unknown rights and explains the reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sulh over an unknown right and explain why jahalah does not invalidate the contract when it involves isqat.

Core Idea
Sulh over unknown rights is valid because ignorance in dropping a right does not lead to dispute.

In this lesson, we explore a key Hanafi principle: a compromise (sulh) where the rights being relinquished are unknown but the compensation is known is perfectly valid. This may seem counterintuitive, as contracts generally require clear subject matter. The rationale lies in the nature of isqat (dropping of rights): ignorance here does not cause the kind of dispute that invalidates a sale or exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? Here, it is a sulh (compromise) where the right being compromised (musalah 'anhu) is unknown (majhool) and the compensation (musalah 'alayhi) is known (ma'loom). The ruling is ja'iz (permissible).
The act is a contract of isqat, not a pure exchange.
2
Locate the dalil
The passage cites the principle from al-Hidayah and commentary. The proof is istihsan based on the observation that 'jahalah fima yasqutu la tufdi ila al-munaza'ah' (ignorance in what is dropped does not lead to dispute).
Istihsan is a Hanafi method of preferring a stronger legal outcome over a strict analogy.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does this prove the ruling? The phrase 'la tufdi ila al-munaza‘ah' indicates the effective cause ('illa): the absence of likely dispute. Since isqat does not require the same precision as an exchange, ignorance is tolerated.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) require knowledge of the subject matter in all contracts. The Hanafi school distinguishes isqat from exchange, allowing ignorance in the former.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is that in Shafi'i such sulh would be invalid.
5
Evaluate the practical consequence
If a third party proves ownership of part of the house, the compensation is divided proportionally because it was a substitute for the whole property and the whole was not delivered.
This is illustrated in the Hidayah example: if 10% is claimed, 10% of the compensation is returned.

Source Text

قَالَ (وَمَنْ ادَّعَى حَقًّا فِي دَارِ) مَنْ ادَّعَى حَقًّا مَجْهُولًا فِي دَارٍ بِيَدِ رَجُلٍ فَصَالَحَهُ الَّذِي فِي يَدِهِ عَلَى مِائَةِ دِرْهَمٍ فَاسْتَحَقَّ الدَّارَ إِلَّا ذِرَاعًا مِنْهَا لَمْ يَرْجِعُ بِشَيْءٍ، لِأَنَّ لِلْمُدَّعِى أَنْ يَقُولَ دَعْوَايَ فِي هَذَا الْبَاقِي، وَإِنْ ادَّعَى كُلَّهَا فَصَالَحَهُ عَلَى مِائَةٍ فَاسْتُحِقَ مِنْهَا شَيْءٌ رَجَعَ بِحِسَابِهِ، إِذِ التَّوْفِيقُ غَيْرُ مُمْكِنٍ، وَالْمِائَةُ كَانَتْ بَدَلًا عَنْ كُلِّ الدَّارِ وَلَمْ تُسَلَّمُ فَتُقْسَمُ الْمِائَةُ، لِأَنَّ الْبَدَلَ يُقْسَمُ عَلَى أَجْزَاءِ الْمُبْدَلِ وَدَلَّتْ الْمَسْأَلَةُ عَلَى أَنَّ الصُّلْحَ عَنِ الْمَجْهُولِ عَلَى الْمَعْلُومِ جَائِزٌ لِأَنَّ الْجَهَالَةَ فِيمَا يَسْقُطُ لَا تُفْضِى إِلَى الْمُنَازَعَةِ). (العناية: كتاب البيوع، باب الاستحقاق، ج ۷ ص ۴۹)
الهداية: كتاب البيوع ، فصل في بيع الفضولي، ج ۳ ص ۹۳
Usool-e-Hidayah Volume Three. (33) Sulh over the unknown for a known is permissible. Translation: Compromise where the thing given in exchange is unknown and the thing received is known is permissible. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that if the musalah ‘anhu (i.e., the thing for which compromise is being made) is unknown and the musalah ‘alayhi (the thing received) is known, then it is permissible. For example, someone claimed an unspecified share in a house, and the possessor made a compromise for a known amount of money, then it is permissible. The author of al-Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if someone claimed the entire house, and the defendant made a compromise with the claimant for one hundred dirhams, then later another rightful claimant appeared for a part of the house, then the defendant will reclaim from the claimant a proportionate amount of the dirhams given. For example, if a ten percent share is claimed, then the possessor will take back ten dirhams from the claimant. Thus, it is established that compromise over an unknown thing for a known consideration is valid. The claimant dropped his right by taking one hundred dirhams, and things that are of the nature of dropping rights, ignorance in them does not lead to dispute. Therefore, compromise over them for a known thing is valid. Remember that for the validity of compromise, the correctness of the claim is not a condition, because here the claimant's claim regarding the house is not valid due to ignorance of the right, yet despite that, the compromise upon it is valid and correct. al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Buyu’, Bab al-Istiḥqaq, vol. 3, p. 93. And the case indicates that compromise over the unknown for a known is permissible, because ignorance in what is dropped does not lead to dispute. And the case also indicates that the validity of the claim is not a condition for the validity of the compromise, because the claim of a right in the house is not valid due to ignorance, and therefore evidence is not accepted for that unless the claimant alleges an admission by the defendant of the right; then the claim becomes valid and evidence and admission regarding the unknown are accepted according to us. (al-Binaya, Kitab al-Buyu’, Bab al-Istiḥqaq, vol. 8, p. 310) He said: Whoever claims a right in a house – whoever claims an unknown right in a house in the possession of a man, then the possessor made a compromise with him for one hundred dirhams, and then the house is found to belong to another except one cubit of it, he does not reclaim anything, because the claimant can say: my claim is for this remaining (portion). And if he claimed all of it and made a compromise for one hundred, and then some of it is claimed, he reclaims proportionately, because reconciliation is not possible, and the one hundred was a substitute for the entire house and it was not delivered, so the one hundred is divided, because the substitute is divided over the parts of the thing substituted. And the case indicates that compromise over the unknown for the known is permissible, because ignorance in what is dropped does not lead to dispute. (al-‘Inayah, Kitab al-Buyu’, Bab al-Istiḥqaq, vol. 7, p. 49) al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Buyu’, Fasl fi Bay’ al-Fuduli, vol. 3, p. 93.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a combination of the Urdu commentary (Usool-e-Hidayah) and the original Arabic text from al-Hidayah and other commentaries. The passage establishes a key Hanafi principle: compromise (sulh) over an unknown thing (majhool) for a known consideration (ma‘loom) is valid. The reasoning is that when the subject matter involves dropping a right (isqat), ignorance (jahalah) does not necessarily lead to dispute (niza‘), unlike in contracts of exchange where ignorance can cause dispute. The passage also clarifies that the validity of the underlying claim is not a condition for the validity of the compromise. Even if the claim itself is invalid due to ignorance, the compromise can still be valid. The detailed case from the Hidayah illustrates this: if someone claims an unspecified right in a house, the possessor can make a compromise for a fixed amount. If later a third party proves ownership of part of the house, the compromise amount is divided proportionally because the substitute (badal) is divided over the parts of the thing substituted (mubdal). This shows that the ruling is based on the principle that ignorance in matters of dropping rights does not cause dispute, hence the sulh is valid.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A compromise or settlement where a claimant drops their right in exchange for compensation, distinct from a pure gift or sale.
مجہول (majhool): Unknown or unspecified; here refers to the right claimed which is not precisely defined (e.g., an unspecified share of a house).
اسقاط (isqat): Dropping or relinquishing a right, such as dropping a claim in compromise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a compromise (sulh) over an unknown right for a known consideration valid?
A) Because all contracts require complete knowledge of the subject matter.
B) Because ignorance in matters of dropping rights does not lead to dispute.
C) Because the claim must be valid for the compromise to be valid.
D) Because the compromise is considered a sale.

Key Takeaway
Sulh over an unknown right is valid in the Hanafi school because the ignorance pertains to a right being dropped, not an exchange, and thus does not cause dispute.

Subsequent Permission is Like Prior Agency
Explaining the principle that subsequent permission validates like prior agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle that subsequent permission validates a fuduli contract retroactively, and apply it to a real scenario involving an unauthorized sale.

Core Idea
Subsequent permission makes the fuduli an agent retroactively.

This passage from Usool-e-Hidayah introduces a foundational Hanafi principle: when an unauthorized person sells another's property (bay' fuduli), the contract is suspended (mawquf) until the owner grants permission. If permission is given later, it is as if the fuduli was an agent from the beginning. This lesson unpacks the principle, its legal reasoning, and its practical applications in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a sale by an unauthorized person (fuduli). Initially, the contract is suspended (mawquf) — neither valid nor void. The ruling depends on the owner's subsequent action: if he ratifies, the contract becomes valid (sahih); if he rejects, it becomes void (batil).
This suspension is unique to Hanafi fiqh.
2
State the principle
The passage states: 'Subsequent permission has the status of prior agency' (al-ijarah al-lahiqa bi-manzilati al-wakalati al-sabiqati). This means that the owner's later ratification has the same legal effect as appointing an agent beforehand.
3
Explain the reasoning (ta'lil)
The principle is based on istihsan (juristic preference) favoring the validity of transactions. Permission retroactively validates the contract, making the fuduli akin to an agent. The underlying logic is that the owner's consent, whether given before or after, achieves the same goal.
The alternative (qiyas) would require voiding such contracts, causing hardship.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that permission is necessary; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad agree in principle but differ on details, such as whether the buyer acquires ownership from the moment of contract or from the moment of permission. The mufta bihi position is that the contract is valid from the beginning.
The difference affects the timing of ownership transfer.
5
Apply the principle to a scenario
Consider A sells B's car to C without B's permission. B later says 'I approve.' According to the principle, the sale is valid from the time A and C contracted, and A is treated as B's agent. The car becomes C's property retroactively, and C must pay the price to B.
This facilitates commerce by allowing owners to validate beneficial deals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيع الفضولي
(۳۴) الْإِجَارَةُ اللَّاحِقَةُ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْوَكَالَةِ السَّابِقَةِ.
ترجمہ: اجازت لاحقہ وکالت سابقہ کے درجہ میں ہے۔
۶۷
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ بیع فضولی میں جب اصل مالک اجازت دے دے تو یہ فضولی بمنزلہ وکیل کے ہو گا ، اور جو احکامات وکالت میں ملحوظ ہوتے ہیں وہ اس فضولی کے بیچ کی صورت میں بھی ملحوظ ہوں گے۔ اور مالک کا بعد میں اجازت دینا ایسا ہے جیسا کہ اُس نے اسے پہلے سے وکیل بنایا ہو۔
الهداية: كتاب البيوع ، فصل في بيع الفضولي، ج ۳ ص ۹۳
Chapter on the sale of the unauthorized person. (34) Subsequent permission has the status of prior agency. Translation: Subsequent permission is equivalent to prior agency. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that in the sale by an unauthorized person (fuduli), when the original owner grants permission, then this unauthorized person becomes like an agent, and the rulings that are considered in agency will also be considered in the case of the sale by this unauthorized person. And the owner's subsequent permission is like him having appointed him as an agent beforehand. al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Buyu’, Fasl fi Bay’ al-Fuduli, vol. 3, p. 93.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is from Usool-e-Hidayah explaining a key principle of Hanafi fiqh regarding the contract of an unauthorized person (fuduli). The principle states that subsequent permission (ijazah lahiqah) has the same legal effect as prior agency (wakalah sabiqah). In Hanafi law, if someone sells another's property without authorization (bay' fuduli), the contract is suspended on the owner's ratification. If the owner subsequently gives permission, the contract becomes valid and effective from the time of the contract, and the unauthorized person is treated as if he had been an agent from the outset. This means all the rules of agency apply, including the rights and obligations regarding the parties and the property. The underlying reasoning (ta'lil) is that permission retroactively validates the contract, making it as if the agent had prior authorization. This principle is crucial for understanding the validity of unauthorized transactions and the role of ratification in Hanafi law.

Key Terms
اجازت (ijazah): Permission or ratification given by the owner to validate a contract made by an unauthorized person.
فضولی (fuduli): An unauthorized person who acts on behalf of another without prior permission, e.g., selling someone else's property.
وکالت (wakalah): Agency; the legal relationship where one person (principal) authorizes another (agent) to act on his behalf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle explained in this lesson, what is the effect of an owner's subsequent permission on a sale made by an unauthorized person (fuduli)?
A) The contract becomes void because the agent lacked authority.
B) The contract is validated and the fuduli is treated as an agent retroactively.
C) The contract requires a new agreement between the parties.
D) The fuduli must pay compensation to the owner.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that subsequent permission equals prior agency is a cornerstone of transaction law, allowing unauthorized contracts to be ratified and treated as valid from the start.

Sulh Over an Unknown Right: Validity and Conditions
Ruling with case-based proof on sulh over unknown right

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sulh over an unknown right and apply it to a hypothetical dispute case.

Core Idea
Sulh over an unknown right is valid because ignorance in what is waived does not lead to dispute.

Compromise (sulh) is a valuable tool for resolving disputes, but what if the right being claimed is unknown? Classical Hanafi fiqh addresses this directly: knowledge of the exact right is not a condition for a valid sulh. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the case-based proof, and the reasoning that distinguishes the Hanafi position from other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Determine the legal act under discussion (sulh) and its ruling (valid/mubah). Note that the ruling applies when the claimed right is unknown but the consideration is known.
2
Analyze the case proof
Examine the example given: claimant with an unknown right in a house settles for 100 dirhams; later, he is found to own all but one cubit. Trace the reasoning that he keeps the full amount because his claim can be interpreted as covering the remaining part.
This demonstrates the principle that jihalat (ignorance) in what is waived does not cause harm.
3
Extract the legal principle
From the case, derive the general rule: sulh over an unknown right for a known consideration is valid, and jihalat in the waived right is tolerated because it does not lead to further dispute.
4
Note the cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
Identify that other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) require the subject of sulh to be known to avoid gharar. Understand the thamarah al-khilaf: in practice, Hanafis allow sulh where others would require specification.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Recognize that the Hanafi position is mufta bihi in the school, supported by the reasoning that ignorance in what is waived is benign.
The principle is part of Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليست بشرط لصحة الصلح، لأن دعوى الحق في الدار لا تصح للجهالة، ولهذا لا تقبل البينة على ذلك إلا إذا ادعى إقرار المدعى عليه بالحق فحينئذ تصح الدعوى وتقبل البيئة والإقرار عن المجهول جائز عندنا البناية كتاب البيوع، باب الاستحقاق، ج۸ ص ۳۱۰)
قَالَ (وَمَنْ ادَّعَى حَقًّا فِي دَارِ) مَنْ ادَّعَى حَقًّا مَجْهُولًا فِي دَارٍ بِيَدِ رَجُلٍ فَصَالَحَهُ الَّذِي فِي يَدِهِ عَلَى مِائَةِ دِرْهَمٍ فَاسْتَحَقَّ الدَّارَ إِلَّا ذِرَاعًا مِنْهَا لَمْ يَرْجِعُ بِشَيْءٍ، لِأَنَّ لِلْمُدَّعِى أَنْ يَقُولَ دَعْوَايَ فِي هَذَا الْبَاقِي، وَإِنْ ادَّعَى كُلَّهَا فَصَالَحَهُ عَلَى مِائَةٍ فَاسْتُحِقَ مِنْهَا شَيْءٌ رَجَعَ بِحِسَابِهِ، إِذِ التَّوْفِيقُ غَيْرُ مُمْكِنٍ، وَالْمِائَةُ كَانَتْ بَدَلًا عَنْ كُلِّ الدَّارِ وَلَمْ تُسَلَّمُ فَتُقْسَمُ الْمِائَةُ، لِأَنَّ الْبَدَلَ يُقْسَمُ عَلَى أَجْزَاءِ الْمُبْدَلِ وَدَلَّتْ الْمَسْأَلَةُ عَلَى أَنَّ الصُّلْحَ عَنْ الْمَجْهُولِ عَلَى الْمَعْلُومِ جَائِزٌ لِأَنَّ الْجَهَالَةَ فِيمَا يَسْقُطُ لَا تُفْضِى إِلَى الْمُنَازَعَةِ). (العناية: كتاب البيوع، باب الاستحقاق، ج ۷ ص ۴۹)
It is not a condition for the validity of sulh, because a claim of a right in a house is not valid due to ignorance, and for this reason evidence is not accepted for it except if he claims an acknowledgment from the defendant that he has a right; then the claim is valid and evidence is accepted, and acknowledgment concerning an unknown (right) is permissible according to us. (al-Binaayah, Kitab al-Buyu, Bab al-Istiqaq, vol. 8, p. 310) He said: If someone claims an unknown right in a house in the possession of a man, and the possessor makes a compromise with him for one hundred dirhams, then the claimant proves ownership of the house except for a cubit of it, he gets nothing back, because the claimant can say: My claim is in this remaining part. But if he claimed all of it and made a compromise for one hundred, then part of it is established as his, he gets back proportionally, because reconciliation (between the claim and the reality) is impossible. The one hundred was in exchange for the entire house and it was not delivered, so the one hundred is divided, because the exchange is divided over the parts of the exchange item. This case indicates that sulh over an unknown (right) for a known consideration is valid, because ignorance in what is waived does not lead to dispute. (al-'Inayah, Kitab al-Buyu, Bab al-Istiqaq, vol. 7, p. 49)
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a compromise (sulh) when the claimed right is unknown. It begins by stating that knowledge of the exact right is not a condition for the validity of sulh. This is because a claim of an unknown right in a property is not itself valid for litigation, but if the defendant acknowledges that the claimant has a right (without specifying), then the claim becomes valid and acknowledgment is accepted. The text then presents a case from al-Hidayah: A person claims an unknown right in a house owned by another. They settle for 100 dirhams. Later, it is established that the claimant actually owns the entire house except for one cubit. In that case, the claimant does not return any part of the 100 dirhams because his claim could be interpreted as covering the remaining part. However, if he claimed the entire house and settled for 100, and only part is established, he must return a proportionate amount of the 100. This is because the 100 was payment for the whole house, and since the whole was not delivered, the payment is divided according to the portion established. The lesson concludes that sulh over an unknown right for a known consideration is valid, as ignorance pertains to what is being waived (the right), which does not lead to future dispute.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A compromise or settlement of a dispute by mutual agreement, often involving a payment.
جهالة (jihalat): Ignorance or uncertainty about the specific nature or extent of a right or property.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment or admission by the defendant that the claimant has a right, even if unspecified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling discussed, what is the outcome when a claimant settles for a known sum over an unspecified right in property, and later part of the property is proven to be his, but not all?
A) The claimant must return the entire sum.
B) The claimant keeps the entire sum.
C) The claimant returns a proportionate amount.
D) The sulh is invalidated.

Key Takeaway
Sulh over an unknown right is valid in the Hanafi school because the ignorance pertains to the right being waived, which does not lead to further dispute.

Subsequent Permission in Fuduli Sale Is Equivalent to Prior Agency
States the legal principle that subsequent permission in fuduli sale serves as prior agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle of subsequent permission in fuduli sale and identify the conditions required for its validity.

Core Idea
After permission, the fuduli becomes like a wakil — the price is the owner's property held as a trust.

In Hanafi fiqh, when an unauthorized person (fuduli) sells another's property, the owner's later permission can validate the sale retroactively. This lesson explores the legal maxim that such subsequent permission (ijazah lahiqah) is equivalent to prior agency (wakalah sabiqah), the conditions for its effectiveness, and the resulting consequences for the price and the fuduli's liability.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its legal effect
The passage opens with the maxim: 'Subsequent permission is in the status of prior agency.' This means that when the owner grants permission after a fuduli sale, the fuduli becomes like a wakil (agent) from the beginning. Consequently, the price becomes the owner's property and is held as an amanah (trust) by the fuduli.
2
Identify the type of sale to which this maxim applies
The text distinguishes two types of fuduli sales: (1) where the price is a debt (thaman dayn) such as dirhams, dinars, or generic measurable items, and (2) where the price is a specific item (thaman 'ayn). The maxim applies only to the first type. For the second type, different rules may apply.
This distinction is crucial to avoid applying the principle universally.
3
List the four conditions for the permission to be valid
For the permission to attach, four elements must still exist at the time of permission: the seller (fuduli), the buyer, the owner, and the sold item (ma'qud 'alayh). If any of these is no longer present, the permission is ineffective.
These conditions ensure the contract can still be ratified.
4
Explain the legal consequence of valid permission
When the owner grants permission with all four conditions met, the sale is validated retroactively. The fuduli is treated as a wakil, and the price is owned by the owner. In the fuduli's hand, the price is an amanah, so if it is lost without the fuduli's negligence, he is not liable.
This is the key practical effect — liability is removed upon permission.
5
Contrast with the scenario where the price is a specific item
The text implies that if the fuduli sells for a specific price (thaman 'ayn), the owner's right to grant subsequent permission may not apply. This limitation is important for proper application of the maxim.
The passage from al-'Inayah mentions both cases but only elaborates the first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيع الفضولي
(۳۴) الْإِجَارَةُ اللَّاحِقَةُ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْوَكَالَةِ السَّابِقَةِ.
ترجمہ: اجازت لاحقہ وکالت سابقہ کے درجہ میں ہے۔
۶۷
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ بیع فضولی میں جب اصل مالک اجازت دے دے تو یہ فضولی بمنزلہ وکیل کے ہو گا ، اور جو احکامات وکالت میں ملحوظ ہوتے ہیں وہ اس فضولی کے بیچ کی صورت میں بھی ملحوظ ہوں گے۔ اور مالک کا بعد میں اجازت دینا ایسا ہے جیسا کہ اُس نے اسے پہلے سے وکیل بنایا ہو۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ فضولی کی بیچ کی صورت میں اگر مالک اجازت دے دے تو شمن مالک کا مملوک ہوگا اور یہ ثمن فضولی کے قبضہ میں امانت ہوگا، جیسے وکیل کے قبضہ میں امانت ہوتا ہے، کیونکہ یہاں مالک نے اجازت دے دی ہے اور جب مالک اجازت دے دے تو فضولی بمنزلہ وکیل کے ہوتا ہے اور وکیل کے قبضے میں مؤکل کا شمن امانت ہوتا ہے، اور ہلاکت کی صورت میں جس طرح وکیل پر کوئی ضمان لازم نہیں ہوتا، اسی طرح فضولی پر بھی کوئی ضمان لازم نہیں ہوگا ، مذکورہ بالا اصول کا خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ بعد میں اجازت دینا ایسا ہی ہے جیسا کہ ابتداء میں کسی کو وکیل بنایا گیا ہو۔
(قوله وله الإجازة إذا كان المعقود عليه باقيا والمتعاقدان بحالهما) واعلم أن قيام الأربعة شرط للحوق الإجازة البائع والمشترى والمالك والمبيع فإن أجازه المالك مع قيام هذه الأربعة جاز وتكون الإجازة اللاحقة بمنزلة الوكالة السابقة ويكون البائع كالوكيل والثمن للمجيز إن كان قائما وإن هلک فی ید البائع هلك أمانة. (الجوهرة النيرة: كتاب البيوع، باب خيار الرؤية، ج ١ ص ١٩٦ )
(قَوْلُهُ وَلَهُ) أَى لِلْمَالِكِ (الْإِجَازَةُ) اعْلَمُ أَنَّ الْفُضُولِيَّ إِمَّا أَنْ يَبِيعَ الْعَيْنَ بِثَمَنِ دَيْنِ كَالدَّرَاهِم وَالدَّنَانِيرِ وَالْفُلُوسِ وَالْكَيْلِيُّ وَالْوَزْنِيُّ الْمَوْصُوفَ بِغَيْرِ عَيْنِهِ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَبِيعَ بِثَمَنِ عَيْنٍ، فَإِنْ كَانَ الْأَوَّلُ فَلِلْمَالِكِ الْإِجَازَةُ إِذَا كَانَ الْمَعْقُودُ عَلَيْهِ بَاقِيَا وَالْمُتَعَاقِدَانِ بِحَالِهِمَا، فَإِنْ أَجَازَ حَالَ قِيَامِ الْأَرْبَعَةِ جَازَ الْبَيْعُ لِمَا ذُكِرَ أَنَّ الْإِجَازَةَ تُصْرَفْ فِى الْعَقْدِ فَلا بد مِنْ قِيَامِهِ وَذَلِكَ بِقِيَامِ الْعَاقِدَيْنِ وَالْمَعْقُودِ عَلَيْهِ وَكَانَتْ الْإِجَازَةُ اللَّاحِقَةُ كَالْوَ كَالَةِ السَّابِقَةِ فَيَكُونَ الْبَائِعُ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْوَكِيلِ وَالثَّمَنُ مَمْلُوكٌ لِلْمَالِكِ أَمَانَةٌ فِي يَدِ الْفُضُولِيّ. (العناية: كتاب البيوع، فصل في بيع الفضولي، ج۷ ص ۵۴)
Chapter on the Sale by Fuduli. (34) Subsequent permission is in the status of prior agency. Translation: Subsequent permission is in the status of prior agency. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that in a fuduli sale, when the original owner gives permission, then this fuduli becomes in the status of a wakil, and the rulings that are observed in agency will also be observed in the sale of this fuduli. And the owner's subsequent permission is as if he had appointed him as agent from the beginning. The author of al-Hidayah, after mentioning the above principle, is explaining that in the case of a fuduli sale, if the owner gives permission, then the price becomes owned by the owner, and this price in the possession of the fuduli is amanah, just as in the possession of a wakil it is amanah, because here the owner has given permission, and when the owner gives permission, the fuduli becomes in the status of a wakil, and in the possession of a wakil, the price of the principal is amanah, and in case of loss, just as no guarantee is required from the wakil, similarly no guarantee is required from the fuduli. The summary of the above principle is that subsequent permission is the same as if someone had been appointed as agent from the beginning. (His statement: And he has the right to grant permission if the object of contract remains and the two contractors remain in their state.) Know that the existence of four things is a condition for the attachment of permission: the seller, the buyer, the owner, and the sold item. If the owner grants permission while these four exist, it is valid, and subsequent permission is in the status of prior agency, and the seller is like a wakil, and the price belongs to the one who granted permission, if it is extant, and if it perishes in the hand of the seller, it perishes as amanah. (al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah, Kitab al-Buyu, Bab Khiyar al-Ru'yah, vol. 1, p. 196). (His statement: And he) meaning the owner (has the right to grant permission). Know that the fuduli either sells the specific item for a price that is a debt, like dirhams, dinars, fulus, or measurable and weighable items described without specification, or he sells for a specific price. If it is the first, then the owner has the right to grant permission if the object of contract remains and the two contractors are still in their state. If he grants permission while the four exist, the sale is valid because, as mentioned, permission is directed towards the contract, so its existence is necessary, and that is through the existence of the two contractors and the object of contract. And subsequent permission is like prior agency, so the seller is in the status of a wakil, and the price is owned by the owner, being an amanah in the hand of the fuduli. (al-'Inayah, Kitab al-Buyu, Fasl fi Bay' al-Fuduli, vol. 7, p. 54)
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This is a statement of a legal principle (qa'idah) in the Hanafi school concerning the sale by a fuduli (unauthorized person). The principle is that subsequent permission (ijazah lahiqah) from the owner has the same effect as prior agency (wakalah sabiqah). This means that when an unauthorized person sells someone else's property without permission, and later the owner ratifies the sale, the fuduli becomes like an agent (wakil) of the owner from the beginning. Consequently, all rules of agency apply: the price becomes the property of the owner, and in the hand of the fuduli it is held as an amanah (trust). If the price is lost without negligence, the fuduli is not liable. The passage also explains the conditions for the permission to be effective: the sold item must still exist, and both the seller (fuduli) and buyer must still be alive and capable. Additionally, the owner, the seller, the buyer, and the sold item (the four) must all be present at the time of permission. Once these conditions are met, the sale is validated retroactively. The text from al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah and al-'Inayah confirms this, emphasizing that the seller becomes like a wakil and the price is an amanah in his possession.

Key Terms
فضولی (fuduliyy): An unauthorized person who sells property belonging to another without permission.
إجازة لاحقة (ijazah lahiqah): Subsequent permission granted by the owner after the fuduli has already sold the property.
وكالة سابقة (wakalah sabiqah): Prior agency where the owner appoints someone to act on his behalf before the transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the four things that must exist for the owner's subsequent permission to be effective in a fuduli sale?
A) The owner, the fuduli, the buyer, and the price
B) The seller (fuduli), the buyer, the owner, and the sold item
C) The seller (fuduli), the buyer, the price, and the sold item
D) The owner, the price, the sold item, and the witness

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that subsequent permission in a fuduli sale equals prior agency validates the sale retroactively, making the price an amanah in the fuduli's possession.

The Fuduli Sale: Subsequent Permission Like Prior Agency
Explaining that owner's permission after fuduli sale has effect of prior agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four conditions required for the validity of a fuduli sale upon subsequent permission and explain the Hanafi analogy to agency.

Core Idea
Subsequent permission in a fuduli sale is legally equivalent to prior agency, making the price an amanah.

This passage from Al-Hidayah and its commentaries explains a key Hanafi principle: the fuduli (unauthorized seller) can have his sale validated by the owner's later permission. The ruling hinges on four conditions and a powerful analogy (qiyas) to a principal-agent relationship. Understanding this helps clarify the Hanafi approach to contracts and trust.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Question
The passage addresses whether a sale executed by a fuduli (someone who sells another's property without permission) can be validated by the owner's subsequent permission.
2
State the Hanafi Ruling
The owner may ratify the sale if four conditions are present. Upon ratification, the sale is valid (sahih), the price becomes the owner's property, and the fuduli holds it as a trust (amanah).
3
List the Four Conditions
The text in Al-Inaya and Al-Jawharat al-Nayyira states that for the permission to be effective, the seller, buyer, owner, and sold object must still exist (be present/unchanged) at the time of permission.
If any of these four changes (e.g., buyer dies, object perishes), the permission is invalid.
4
Trace the Qiyas to Agency
The subsequent permission is compared to prior agency: just as an agent (wakil) receives the principal's consent in advance, the fuduli receives it later. The permission retroactively makes the fuduli like an agent, so the price is a trust (amanah) in his hands.
This is a clear qiyas jali (obvious analogy) based on the common element of owner's consent.
5
Note the Practical Consequence
If the price perishes in the fuduli's hand without his fault, it perishes as a trust (no liability), exactly as with a wakil. This protects the fuduli from undue responsibility.

Source Text

اصول بدایہ ثالث
YA
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ فضولی کی بیچ کی صورت میں اگر مالک اجازت دے دے تو شمن مالک کا مملوک ہوگا اور یہ ثمن فضولی کے قبضہ میں امانت ہوگا، جیسے وکیل کے قبضہ میں امانت ہوتا ہے، کیونکہ یہاں مالک نے اجازت دے دی ہے اور جب مالک اجازت دے دے تو فضولی بمنزلہ وکیل کے ہوتا ہے اور وکیل کے قبضے میں مؤکل کا شمن امانت ہوتا ہے، اور ہلاکت کی صورت میں جس طرح وکیل پر کوئی ضمان لازم نہیں ہوتا، اسی طرح فضولی پر بھی کوئی ضمان لازم نہیں ہوگا ، مذکورہ بالا اصول کا خلاصہ یہ ہے
کہ بعد میں اجازت دینا ایسا ہی ہے جیسا کہ ابتداء میں کسی کو وکیل بنایا گیا ہو۔
(قوله وله الإجازة إذا كان المعقود عليه باقيا والمتعاقدان بحالهما) واعلم أن قيام الأربعة شرط للحوق الإجازة البائع والمشترى والمالك والمبيع فإن أجازه المالك مع قيام هذه الأربعة جاز وتكون الإجازة اللاحقة بمنزلة الوكالة السابقة ويكون البائع كالوكيل والثمن للمجيز إن كان قائما وإن هلک فی ید البائع هلك أمانة. (الجوهرة النيرة: كتاب البيوع، باب خيار الرؤية، ج ١ ص ١٩٦ )
(قَوْلُهُ وَلَهُ) أَى لِلْمَالِكِ (الْإِجَازَةُ) اعْلَمُ أَنَّ الْفُضُولِيَّ إِمَّا أَنْ يَبِيعَ الْعَيْنَ بِثَمَنِ دَيْنِ كَالدَّرَاهِم وَالدَّنَانِيرِ وَالْفُلُوسِ وَالْكَيْلِيُّ وَالْوَزْنِيُّ الْمَوْصُوفَ بِغَيْرِ عَيْنِهِ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَبِيعَ بِثَمَنِ عَيْنٍ، فَإِنْ كَانَ الْأَوَّلُ فَلِلْمَالِكِ الْإِجَازَةُ إِذَا كَانَ الْمَعْقُودُ عَلَيْهِ بَاقِيَا وَالْمُتَعَاقِدَانِ بِحَالِهِمَا، فَإِنْ أَجَازَ حَالَ قِيَامِ الْأَرْبَعَةِ جَازَ الْبَيْعُ لِمَا ذُكِرَ أَنَّ الْإِجَازَةَ تُصْرَفْ فِى الْعَقْدِ فَلا بد مِنْ قِيَامِهِ وَذَلِكَ بِقِيَامِ الْعَاقِدَيْنِ وَالْمَعْقُودِ عَلَيْهِ وَكَانَتْ الْإِجَازَةُ اللَّاحِقَةُ كَالْوَ كَالَةِ السَّابِقَةِ فَيَكُونُ الْبَائِعُ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْوَكِيلِ وَالثَّمَنُ مَمْلُوكٌ لِلْمَالِكِ أَمَانَةٌ فِي يَدِ الْفُضُولِيّ. (العناية: كتاب البيوع، فصل في بيع الفضولي، ج۷ ص ۵۴)
(His statement: 'And he has the right to permission when the object of contract remains and the two contractors are unchanged.') Know that the existence of four things is a condition for the validity of the permission: the seller, the buyer, the owner, and the sold object. If the owner permits while these four exist, it is valid, and the subsequent permission is like prior agency. Then the seller is like an agent, and the price belongs to the owner; if it is present, it is a trust in the hand of the seller, and if it perishes in the seller’s hand, it perishes as a trust. (Al-Jawharat al-Nayyira: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab Khiyar al-Ru'ya, Vol. 1, p. 196)
(His statement: 'And he has') i.e., the owner ('the permission.' Know that the fuduli either sells the specific property for a price that is a debt, such as dirhams, dinars, fulus, and measurable or weighable goods described without specification, or he sells for a specific price. If it is the first, then the owner has the right to permission when the object of contract is still extant and the two contractors remain as they were. If he permits with the existence of the four, the sale is valid for what was mentioned: that the permission pertains to the contract, so its existence is necessary, which requires the existence of the two contractors and the object of contract. And the subsequent permission is like prior agency, so the seller is like an agent, and the price is owned by the owner and is a trust in the hand of the fuduli. (Al-Inaya: Kitab al-Buyu', Fasl fi Bay' al-Fuduli, Vol. 7, p. 54)
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the fuduli (unauthorized agent) sale. The author explains that if a person sells another's property without permission (fuduli), the owner may subsequently ratify the sale. Upon ratification, the price becomes the owner's property and is held in trust by the fuduli, just as an agent (wakil) holds the principal's property in trust. The key condition is that the four elements must persist at the time of ratification: the seller (fuduli), the buyer, the owner, and the sold object itself. If any of these change (e.g., the buyer dies or the object perishes), the ratification is invalid. The proof is based on the analogy (qiyas) to agency: the subsequent permission is legally equivalent to prior appointment as an agent. Thus, the fuduli is treated like a wakil, and the price in his hands is an amanah (trust), not a liability (daman). If it perishes without his fault, he is not liable. This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, as stated in zahir al-riwayah texts like Al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
الفضولي (al-fuduli): A person who sells another's property without prior permission.
الإجازة (al-ijazah): Subsequent permission from the owner ratifying a contract made by a fuduli.
أمانة (amanah): A trust; property held without liability for accidental loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the four conditions required for the owner's permission to validate a fuduli sale?
A) Seller, buyer, object, price
B) Seller, buyer, owner, sold object
C) Owner, agent, contract, receipt
D) Mutual consent, written contract, witnesses, delivery

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a fuduli sale is validated by the owner's subsequent permission provided the seller, buyer, owner, and sold object remain unchanged; the fuduli is then treated as an agent and the price as a trust.

The Principle of Presumption in Salam: Wahm Treated as Reality
Stating a legal principle treating presumption as reality in salam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that treats presumption as actual in salam and apply it to determine the validity of a salam contract when there is doubt about the capital.

Core Idea
In salam, a plausible doubt about the capital is treated as certain defect, to preserve clarity in a concessional contract.

This lesson introduces a unique Hanafi principle specific to the salam (advance payment) contract: any plausible suspicion about the capital being defective is legally treated as if it were actually defective. This principle underpins Imam Abu Hanifa's requirement that the quantity of capital must be precisely known. Let's examine why the Hanafi school imposes this strict condition despite salam being an exceptional concession.

Guided Path
1
State the principle
Read the Arabic maxim and its translation: 'al-mawhum fi bab al-salam ka al-muhaqqaq'. Understand that it means a presumption of defect in the capital is given the ruling of actual defect.
Commit the Arabic phrase to memory; it is a key rule for salam.
2
Identify the context of salam
Recall that salam is a sale where the price (capital) is paid immediately but the goods are delivered later. It is an exception to the general rule against selling non-existent items, allowed by Quran and Sunnah as a concession.
Salam is based on rukhsah (concession), so its conditions are strict to prevent disputes.
3
Explain the rationale
Since salam is an exception, any uncertainty that could lead to conflict must be eliminated. A plausible doubt about the capital's soundness is treated as actual because it undermines the certainty of the transaction, which is essential for this concessionary contract.
The text says: 'because salam is based on rukhshah and was legislated despite contravention, the presumed is given the status of the realized.'
4
Apply to Imam Abu Hanifa's condition
Imam Abu Hanifa requires that the quantity of capital be known. If some dirhams might be counterfeit and cannot be replaced immediately, the total amount of valid capital becomes uncertain. Because the presumption of counterfeit is treated as actual, the contract would be invalid if such a doubt exists.
This condition goes beyond the five agreed conditions; it is specific to Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب السلم
(۳۵) الْمَوْهُوْمُ فِى بَابِ السَّلَمِ كَالْمُتَحَقِّقِ .
ترجمہ: بیع سلم کے باب میں موہوم تحقیق کی طرح ہے۔
۶۹
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ بیع سلم کے باب میں شمن کے کھوٹے ہونے کا وہم ایسا ہے جیسا کہ حقیقت میں ثمن کھوٹے ہوں، یعنی جس طرح حقیقت میں ثمن زیوف
بیع سلم کے جواز کے لئے مانع ہے، اسی طرح شمن کے زیوف ہونے کا وہم بھی مانع ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ امام صاحب کے نزدیک پیچ سلم سات شرائط کے بغیر جائز نہیں ہے، ان سات میں سے پانچ میں اتفاق ہے، البتہ امام صاحب کے نزدیک دو اور بھی ضروری ہیں، راس المال کی مقدار معلوم ہو اور دوسری مسلم فیہ کے سپرد کرنے کی جگہ معلوم ہو۔ راس المال کی مقدار کا معلوم ہونا اس لیے ضروری ہے کہ بعض دفعہ کچھ در اہم کھوٹے نکل آتے ہیں اور مجلس عقد میں بدلے نہیں جاتے ، اب اگر راس المال کی مقدار معلوم نہیں ہوگی تو معلوم نہیں ہو سکے گا کتنی مقدار ادا ہوئی ہے اور کتنی مقدار باقی ہے، راس المال میں بعض درا ہم کا کھونا ہونا اگر چہ امر موہوم ہے، لیکن بیچ مسلم میں اسے متفق کا درجہ دیا گیا ہے۔ قیاس کا اگر چہ مقاضا یہ ہے کہ بیچ مسلم جائز نہ ہو اس لیے کہ اس میں مریچ معدوم ہوتی ہے لیکن نصوص کی وجہ سے قیاس کو ترک کیا گیا ہے، چونکہ بیع سلم کا مدار رخصت پر ہے اور منافی کے باوجود اسے مشروع قرار دیا گیا ہے، اس لیے اس میں امر موہوم کو امر محقق کا درجہ دیا گیا ہے۔
الهداية كتاب البيوع، باب السلم، ج ۳ ص ۰۰
Chapter of Salam
(35) The presumed in the chapter of salam is like the realized.
Translation: In the chapter of salam, the presumed is like the realized.
69
Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that in the chapter of salam, the presumption that the price (thaman) is counterfeit (zuyuf) is like the actual fact that the price is counterfeit. That is, just as the actuality of the price being counterfeit prevents the validity of salam, similarly the presumption of it being counterfeit also prevents it. The author of Al-Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, salam is not valid without seven conditions. Five of these are agreed upon, but according to Imam, two additional conditions are necessary: (1) the quantity of the capital (ra's al-mal) must be known, and (2) the place of delivery of the muslam fihi must be known. The quantity of capital must be known because sometimes some dirhams turn out to be counterfeit and cannot be exchanged in the session. If the quantity is unknown, it cannot be determined how much has been paid and how much remains. The presence of counterfeit dirhams in the capital is a matter of presumption (wahm), but in salam it is given the status of certainty. By qiyas (analogy), salam would not be permissible because the subject matter is non-existent at the time, but due to texts (nusus), qiyas is abandoned. Since salam is based on concession (rukhsah) and was legislated despite the contravention, the presumed is given the status of the realized. (Al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Salam, Vol. 3, p. ??)
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This is a definition-ruling passage presenting a legal principle (qa'ida) specific to the salam contract: 'The presumed in salam is like the realized.' The principle means that if there is a possibility that the capital (ra's al-mal) delivered in a salam contract might contain counterfeit coins, that possibility is treated as an actual defect, thus invalidating the contract if the capital is in debt form (e.g., dirhams, dinars). The reason is that salam is a concessional contract (rukhsah) allowed by texts despite the general prohibition of selling non-existent items (ma'dum). Because it is a concession, any doubt that could lead to dispute is taken seriously and given the weight of certainty. The author of Al-Hidayah uses this principle to explain Imam Abu Hanifa's requirement that the quantity of capital must be precisely known. If some coins might be counterfeit and cannot be replaced in the session, the unknown quantity leads to unresolved potential loss, which is akin to actual loss. This shows that in salam, the Hanafi school insists on strict conditions to avoid uncertainty, even though by qiyas, salam itself is an exception. The principle underscores the school's care in maintaining clarity in transactions.

Key Terms
الموهوم (al-mawhum): In salam, a presumption or doubt about a defect in the capital that is given the legal status of actual certainty.
السلم (al-salam): An advance payment sale where the price (ra's al-mal) is paid in cash and the goods (muslam fihi) are delivered later.
زيوف (zuyuf): Counterfeit or defective coins that are not accepted as valid currency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the presumption of counterfeit dirhams in the capital of a salam contract treated as actual defect?
A) Because salam is a concession (rukhsah), so strictness is applied
B) Because all dirhams are typically counterfeit
C) Because Abu Hanifa disliked salam altogether
D) Because the buyer can always reject delivery

Key Takeaway
In salam, a plausible presumption about the capital is legally treated as actual defect to maintain certainty and avoid dispute.

intermediate
6 min
The Presumed in Salam is Like the Actual
Explains the maxim and its application to the condition of knowing capital quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-mawhum fi bab al-salam ka al-mutahaqqiq' to identify the conditions for valid salam and explain the rationale behind Imam Abu Hanifa's additional condition regarding knowledge of the capital amount.

Core Idea
In salam, a mere presumption of defect is treated as an actual defect because the contract is a concession contrary to qiyas.

This lesson introduces a unique Hanafi principle in the salam (forward sale) contract: the presumed is treated as the actual. You will learn why Imam Abu Hanifa requires the exact quantity of the capital to be known, even though the possibility of counterfeit dirhams is only a presumption. This principle demonstrates how concessions in Islamic law are balanced with precautions against uncertainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its scope
State the Arabic maxim and its translation. Recognize that it applies specifically to salam contracts, not all sales.
The maxim is: الْمَوْهُوْمُ فِى بَابِ السَّلَمِ كَالْمُتَحَقِّقِ
2
Understand the problem of counterfeit dirhams
Consider a salam contract where some capital dirhams may be counterfeit. If the quantity is unknown, returning counterfeits reduces the capital paid, but the remaining amount is uncertain, leading to disputes.
This is the practical scenario that triggers the maxim.
3
Apply the maxim to derive the condition
Since the possibility of counterfeit is a presumed defect (mawhum), it is treated as actual. Therefore, knowledge of the capital quantity becomes a condition to prevent uncertainty and dispute.
Imam Abu Hanifa adds this to the five agreed-upon conditions.
4
Recognize why the maxim applies specifically to salam
Salam is a concession (rukhsah) legislated despite being contrary to qiyas. Thus, precautions are stricter: even a presumption is given weight to protect the contract's validity.
This explains the departure from the general principle that matters are judged by certainty.

حقیقت میں ثمن کھوٹے ہوں، یعنی جس طرح حقیقت میں ثمن زیوف بیع سلم کے جواز کے لئے مانع ہے، اسی طرح شمن کے زیوف ہونے کا وہم بھی مانع ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ امام صاحب کے مقدار ادا ہوئی ہے اور کتنی مقدار باقی ہے، راس المال میں بعض درا ہم کا کھونا ہونا اگر چہ امر موہوم ہے، لیکن بیچ مسلم میں اسے متفق کا درجہ دیا گیا ہے۔ قیاس کا اگر چہ مقاضا یہ ہے کہ بیچ مسلم جائز نہ ہو درجہ دیا گیا ہے۔ الهداية كتاب البيوع، باب السلم، ج ۳ ص ۰۰ وَلَهُ أَنَّهُ يُفْضِى إِلَى الْمُنَازَعَةِ لِأَنَّهُ رُبَّمَا يَجِدُ بَعْضَهَا زُيُوفًا وَقَدْ أَنْفَقَ الْبَعْضَ فَيَرُدُّهُ وَلَا يُسْتَبْدَلُ فِى الْمَجْلِسِ، وَفِي الْمِثْلِيَّاتِ يَنْقَسِمُ الْمُسْلَمُ فِيهِ عَلَى قَدْرِ [؟] لا فلا [؟] رَأْسِ الْمَالِ فَيَنْتَقِضُ السَّلَمُ بِقَدْرِ مَا رُدَّ، وَلَا يُدْرَى قَدْرُ الْبَاقِي فَيُفْضِي إِلَى الْمُنَازَعَةِ، وَالْمَوْهُومُ فِى هَذَا الْعَقْدِ كَالْمُتَحَقِّقِ لِشَرْعِيَّتِهِ عَلَى خِلَافِ الْقِيَاسِ. الاختيار لتعليل المختار: كتاب البيوع، باب السلم، ج ۲ ص ۳۵) (فلو لم يعلم قدره وفى بعض النسخ فلو لم يعرف قدره، أى قدر رأس المال لا يدري في كم بقى بعد إخراج الزيوف وإذا كان معلوما بوزن الزيوف، فيعلم في كم انتقص، وتحقيقه أن جهالة قدر رأس المال تستلزم جهالة المسلم فيه، لأن المسلم إليه يتفق رأس المال شيئا فشيئا وربما يجد بعض ذلك زيوفا، ولا يستبدل له في مجلس الرد فيبطل العقد بقدر ما رده. فإذا لم يكن مقدار قدر رأس المال معلوما لا يعلم في كم انتقص السلم، وفي كم بقى فجهالة المسلم فيه مفسدة بالاتفاق، فكذا ما يستلزمها، وهكذا إذا استحق بعض رأس المال ينفسخ العقد بقدره، فإن قيل : ذلك أمر موهوم لا معتبر به فيما بنى على الرخص أجاب المصنف رَحِمَهُ الله عن ذلك بقوله (والموهوم في هذا العقد كالمتحقق الشرعه أى لشرع السلم (مع المنافي) إذ القياس يخالفه. (البناية: كتاب البيوع، باب السلم، ج ۸ ص ۳۴۸، ۳۴۹) الهداية: كتاب البيوع، باب السلم، ج ۳ ص ۱۰۷
The presumed in the chapter of salam is like the actual. Translation: In the chapter of salam, the presumed is like the actual. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that in salam, the presumption that the capital may be counterfeit is like the actual existence of counterfeit capital. Just as actual counterfeit dirhams are an impediment to the validity of salam, similarly, the presumption of counterfeit dirhams is also an impediment. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, salam is not valid without seven conditions. Among these, five are agreed upon, but Imam Abu Hanifa considers two additional conditions necessary: knowledge of the quantity of the capital and knowledge of the place of delivery of the subject matter (muslam fih). Knowning the quantity of the capital is necessary because sometimes some dirhams turn out to be counterfeit and are not exchanged in the contracting council. If the quantity of the capital is not known, it will not be possible to know how much has been paid and how much remains. The existence of some counterfeit dirhams in the capital is a presumed matter, but in salam, it is given the status of actual. Although the requirement of analogy (qiyas) is that salam should not be permissible because the subject matter is non-existent at the time of contract, but due to textual evidences (nusus), analogy is abandoned. Since salam is based on concession (rukhsah) and is legislated despite the existence of a countervailing factor (manafi), a presumed matter is given the status of an actual matter. Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Salam, vol. 3, p. 00. And his (Imam Abu Hanifa's) argument is that it leads to dispute because perhaps he finds some of them (dirhams) counterfeit while he has already spent some, so he returns them but they are not replaced in the council, and in fungible goods, the subject matter (muslam fih) is divided according to the... [؟] otherwise no [؟] ... of the capital, so the salam is annulled to the extent of what is returned, and the amount remaining is not known, leading to dispute. And the presumed in this contract is like the actual because of its legislation contrary to analogy. Al-Ikhtiyar li Ta'lil al-Mukhtar: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Salam, vol. 2, p. 35. (If its amount is not known, and in some copies 'if its value is not known,' meaning the amount of the capital, it is not known how much remains after removing the counterfeit ones. And if it is known by the weight of the counterfeit, then it is known how much is deficient. The verification is that ignorance of the amount of the capital necessitates ignorance of the subject matter (muslam fih), because the buyer (muslam ilayh) pays the capital little by little, and perhaps he finds some of that counterfeit, and he does not exchange it in the council of return, so the contract becomes void to the extent of what he returned. If the amount of the capital is not known, it is not known how much the salam is diminished and how much remains. Ignorance of the subject matter is a source of corruption (mafsadah) by consensus, and likewise what necessitates it. Similarly, if some of the capital is reclaimed (mustahaqq), the contract is rescinded to that extent. If it is said: 'That is a presumed matter, not considered in what is based on concessions,' the author (al-Marghinani) replied with his statement: 'The presumed in this contract is like the actual because of its legislation' i.e., because of the legislation of salam (despite the countervailing factor) since analogy opposes it. (Al-Binayah: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Salam, vol. 8, pp. 348-349) Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Salam, vol. 3, p. 107.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof lesson presenting a key Hanafi legal maxim in the chapter of salam (forward sale): 'The presumed in salam is like the actual.' The passage first states the maxim in Arabic, then provides an Urdu translation and detailed explanation. The commentator explains that the principle means that in a salam contract, the mere presumption or possibility that the capital (ra's al-mal) might contain counterfeit dirhams is treated as if the dirhams were actually counterfeit. This is because the existence of counterfeit dirhams would hinder the validity of the contract, and so does the presumption of their existence. The author of Al-Hidayah uses this principle to introduce Imam Abu Hanifa's additional conditions for the validity of salam: knowledge of the quantity of the capital and knowledge of the place of delivery of the subject matter (muslam fih). These two are in addition to the five conditions agreed upon by all jurists. The necessity of knowing the capital amount arises because if some dirhams turn out to be counterfeit and are not replaced at the council, the exact paid amount becomes uncertain, leading to dispute. This is followed by Arabic excerpts from Al-Hidayah, Al-Ikhtiyar, and Al-Binayah that provide the legal reasoning. The Hidayah text argues that ignorance of the capital amount leads to uncertainty about how much of the subject matter is due, which causes disputes. It emphasizes that the very possibility of counterfeit dirhams is enough to invalidate the contract partially because in this contract, a presumed defect is treated as an actual defect. This is justified by noting that salam is a concessional contract (rukhsah) legislated contrary to qiyas (analogy), so strict precautions are taken. The additional commentary from Al-Ikhtiyar and Al-Binayah further explains that knowledge of the capital amount prevents the corruption (mafsadah) of uncertainty and that the maxim applies specifically because salam is legislated despite its conflict with qiyas.

Key Terms
موہوم (mawhum): A presumed or doubtful matter; in the context of salam, it refers to the suspicion that some of the capital dirhams may be counterfeit.
متحقق (mutahaqqiq): Actual or realized; the opposite of mawhum. In the maxim, it indicates that a presumed defect is given the legal effect of an actual defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Abu Hanifa require the quantity of the capital (ra's al-mal) to be known in a salam contract?
A) Because it is a general requirement in all sales.
B) Because the possibility of counterfeit dirhams leads to dispute, and in salam, presumed defects are treated as actual.
C) Because the capital must be weighed in the contract.
D) Because the Salam contract is only valid if the capital is silver.

Key Takeaway
In salam contracts, the mere possibility of defect in the capital is treated as an actual defect, making knowledge of the capital amount a necessary condition for validity.

Condition for Validity of Salam: Precise Description and Quantity
Ruling statement: condition for salam validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for a valid salam contract and apply it to determine whether a given commodity qualifies as muslam fih.

Core Idea
Salam is only valid if the goods can be precisely described and quantified — uncertainty voids the contract.

Salam is a forward sale where payment is made in advance for future delivery. Because the goods are not present at the contract, their exact specifications must be known to prevent dispute. This lesson presents the core Hanafi principle that only goods capable of precise description (dabt al-sifah) and quantification (ma'rifa al-miqdar) — typically fungibles (mithliyyat) — can be subject to salam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Contract Type
Recognize that this passage addresses salam (forward sale), a special type of sale for future delivery. The core issue is the subject matter (muslam fih) must be precisely defined.
Salam is an exception to the general rule that sold items must be present; hence strict conditions apply.
2
Locate the Key Condition
The passage states two conditions: (1) dabt al-sifah — ability to describe the attributes precisely, and (2) ma'rifa al-miqdar — knowledge of the quantity. These eliminate uncertainty (jahalah) in the subject matter.
The phrase 'كُلُّ مَا أَمْكَنَ ضَبْطُ صِفَتِهِ وَمَعْرِفَةِ مِقْدَارِهِ' encapsulates the rule.
3
Identify the Permissible Category
The Urdu sharh clarifies that this applies to 'dhawaat al-amthal' (fungible goods) — items that are homogeneous and can be substituted without difference, such as grains, oils, and standardized commodities.
Non-fungibles (qimiyyat) like unique artifacts or land are excluded from salam.
4
State the Consequence of Violation
If the subject matter cannot be precisely described and quantified, the salam contract is fasid (defective) and not enforceable. The condition is a shart al-sihha (condition of validity).
Unlike a missing rukn (pillar) which makes the contract batil (void), failure of this condition renders it fasid, which may be corrected if remedied.
5
Apply to a New Scenario
Given a proposed salam on a custom-made furniture piece: if the wood type, finish, dimensions, and design are fully specified, then it meets the condition. If the design is subjective and cannot be precisely described, the contract is not valid.
The test is whether two parties could independently arrive at the same description without ambiguity.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement presenting a core condition for the validity of a salam (forward sale) contract in Hanafi fiqh. It states that salam is only permissible for goods that can be precisely defined in terms of their description (sifah) and quantity (miqdar). The Urdu explanation clarifies that this applies to items that are homogeneous and do not vary significantly, specifically referring to 'dhawaat al-amthal' (fungible goods). Examples include grains, oils, and other commodities that can be described and measured accurately. The passage implies that salam is not valid for unique or heterogeneous items where precise specification is impossible, as such uncertainty (jahalah) would lead to disputes. This principle is fundamental to salam because the contract involves payment for a future delivery of a commodity, and all essential terms must be known to avoid gharar (excessive uncertainty). The additional phrase 'وَمَا' suggests the original text continues with a conditional clause, but the OCR is incomplete.

Key Terms
ضبط صفت (dabt al-sifah): The ability to precisely define and describe the attributes of a commodity, such as type, quality, and grade.
ذوات الامثال (dhawaat al-amthal): Fungible goods that can be substituted for one another without loss of identity, such as grains, oils, and standardized manufactured items.

Validity of Salam Based on Knowable Description and Quantity
States the principle for validity of salam and explains the consequence of unknowable capital

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle for the validity of salam and apply it to distinguish between salam-capable and salam-incapable items.

Core Idea
Salam is valid only for items whose description and quantity can be precisely specified to avoid dispute.

This lesson examines a foundational principle from the Hanafi school regarding the forward sale (salam). The passage establishes that salam is permissible only when the subject matter's description (sifah) and quantity (miqdar) are knowable. The reasoning is simple yet powerful: knowability prevents future dispute (munaza'ah), which would otherwise invalidate the contract. We will walk through the ruling, its evidence based on istihsan, examples from classical texts, and a special case involving defective capital.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle and its condition
Read the first line of the Arabic: 'Kullu ma amkana dabt sifatihi wa ma'rifati miqdarihi jaza al-salam fihi' – everything possible to control its description and quantity, salam is permissible. Note the condition: both description and quantity must be knowable.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The reason is given: 'li-annahu la yufdi ila al-munaza'ah' – because it does not lead to dispute. This is the effective cause (illa). The ruling is based on istihsan, as it departs from the general analogy that prohibits selling non-existent items.
3
Apply to examples
The commentary applies the principle: jewels (dhawat al-qeem) are not suitable because their description cannot be standardized; bricks (if mold is described) are suitable because they become numerically similar. Practice identifying which items qualify.
4
Analyze the sub-issue: defective capital
The passage discusses a scenario where some coins of the capital are defective (zuyoof). If the remaining good portion is unknown, the salam is partially void due to ignorance of the salam goods. This shows that uncertainty in capital affects the contract's validity.
5
Understand the istihsan basis
The ruling is said to be 'ala khilaf al-qiyas' (contrary to analogy). The Hanafi school uses istihsan to permit salam despite the general prohibition of selling non-existent items, for the sake of trade flexibility. Recognize the usul method at work.

Principles of Hidayah, Volume 3
(36) Everything for which it is possible to control its description and to know its quantity, salam is permissible in it, and what is not (possible), then (it is) not.
Translation: Any item whose description can be precisely specified and whose quantity can be known, salam is permissible in it; otherwise, not.
Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that salam is permissible in all those items whose description and quantity can be known and in which there is no difference, such as standard commodities. And as for the capital: then the salam is annulled to the extent of what was returned, and the amount of the remainder is not known, so it leads to dispute. And the hypothetical in this contract is like the actual for its legislation, contrary to analogy.
(From Al-Ikhtiyar: Book of Sales, Chapter of Salam, vol. 2 p. 35)
(If its amount is not known, and in some copies: if its amount is not recognized, i.e., the amount of the capital, it is not known how much remains after removing the defective coins. And if it is known by the weight of the defective coins, then it is known how much was reduced. And the verification is that ignorance of the amount of the capital entails ignorance of the salam goods, because the one obligated to deliver the salam goods receives the capital little by little, and perhaps he finds some of it defective, and he does not replace it in the session of rejection, so the contract becomes void to the extent of what he returned. So if the amount of the capital is not known, it is not known how much the salam was reduced, and how much remains, so ignorance of the salam goods is a corrupting factor by consensus, and likewise whatever entails it. And thus if part of the capital is rightfully claimed, the contract is rescinded to its extent. If it is said: that is a hypothetical matter, not considered in what is built on concessions, the author (may Allah have mercy on him) replied with his statement: 'And the hypothetical in this contract is like the actual, for its legislation contradicts analogy.' (From Al-Binayah: Book of Sales, Chapter of Salam, vol. 8 pp. 348-349)
(Al-Hidayah: Book of Sales, Chapter of Salam, vol. 3 p. 107)

[PAGE_071]
Principles of Hidayah, Volume 3
And if it is not possible to know its description and quantity, then salam is not permissible in them, such as items of value (unique items). The author of Hidayah, by mentioning the aforementioned principle, is indicating that salam is not permissible in jewels, and salam is permissible in raw bricks and baked bricks, provided the mold of the brick is described. Salam is not permissible in jewels because, due to their variability, it is not possible to know their description and quantity. And any item whose description and quantity cannot be known, salam is not permissible in it. Hence, salam will not be permissible in jewels either. Contrary to bricks: if their mold, size, and quantity are described, the salam contract is valid in them, because after describing the mold, bricks become numerically similar, and in numerically similar items, salam is valid. Therefore, salam is valid in bricks.

(What is possible to control its description and to know its capacity, salam is valid in it, because it does not lead to dispute. And what is not (possible), i.e., what is not possible to control its description and to know its capacity, salam is not valid in it, because it leads to dispute. (From Al-Bahr al-Raiq: Book of Sales, Chapter of Salam, vol. 6 p. 169)
And everything for which it is possible to control its description and to know its quantity, salam is permissible in it, because it does not lead to dispute. And what is not possible to control nor to know its quantity, salam is not permissible in it, because it is unknown and leads to dispute. (From Al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah: Book of Sales, Chapter of Salam, vol. 8 p. 220)
(Al-Hidayah: Book of Sales, Chapter of Salam, vol. 3 p. 107)
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational principle of the Hanafi school regarding the forward sale (salam). The ruling states that salam is valid only for items whose description (sifah) and quantity (miqdar) can be precisely specified. The reason given is that such specification prevents dispute (munaza'ah), which would invalidate the contract. The commentary then applies this principle to examples: jewels (unique, variable items) are not suitable for salam because their description cannot be adequately standardized, while bricks (if their mold is described) are suitable because they become similar to each other. The passage also discusses a sub-issue: if the capital paid in a salam contract contains defective coins (zuyoof) and the quantity of the remaining legitimate capital is unknown, the contract is partially void due to ignorance of the salam goods. The commentator notes that this ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) and is contrary to qiyas (analogy). The principle serves to ensure that the subject matter of the salam is as well-defined as possible, avoiding the uncertainty that would lead to invalidating disputes.

Key Terms
السلم (al-salam): A forward sale where the price is paid in advance for specified goods to be delivered later.
ضبط صفته (dabt sifatihi): The ability to precisely specify or control the description of the item.
ذوات الامثال (dhawat al-amthal): Standard commodities that have uniform substitutes, such as grains or bricks.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To ensure the seller has the goods on hand
B) To comply with a Quranic verse
C) To prevent dispute (munaza'ah)
D) To make the contract equivalent to spot sales

Key Takeaway
Salam is valid only when the subject matter's description and quantity are knowable, preventing uncertainty that would invalidate the contract.

The Legal Maxim: Non-Existent Considered Existent in Legal Effect
States a legal maxim that non-existent can be treated as existent in legal effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim al-ma'dum yu'tabaru mawjudan hukman and apply it to salam and istisna' contracts.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a non-existent object can be legally treated as existent for specific contracts like salam.

The Hanafi school recognizes that under certain circumstances, the law may treat something that does not physically exist as if it exists. This lesson presents this key maxim and its primary application in salam (forward sale), where the goods being sold are not yet in existence at the time of contracting.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the Maxim
Read the Arabic maxim and its translation carefully. The exact wording is the foundation for understanding its application.
Focus on the term 'حكمًا' (hukman) which means 'in legal effect'.
2
Understand the Literal Meaning
The maxim states that something physically non-existent (ma'dum) can be considered existent (mawjud) for legal purposes (hukman). This is a legal fiction.
This does not mean the thing actually exists, but the law treats it as if it does for specific rulings.
3
Identify the Application: Salam
In a salam contract, the seller receives immediate payment but delivers goods later. At the time of contract, the goods do not exist. The maxim allows the contract to be valid by deeming the goods existent in legal effect.
This is an exception to the general rule that one cannot sell what one does not possess.
4
Note the Scope and Limitation
This principle is not general; it applies only to contracts where the law explicitly recognizes a need, such as salam and istisna' (manufacturing contract). It operates based on istihsan (juristic preference).
Other schools like Shafi'i do not accept this principle to the same extent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۳۷) الْمَعْدُومُ قَدْ يُعْتَبَرُ مَوْجُودًا حُكْمًا.
ترجمہ: معدوم کو بھی حکما موجود مان لیا جاتا ہے۔
(37) The non-existent may be considered existent in legal effect.
Translation: The non-existent is also considered existent legally.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents a legal maxim (qa'idah) in the Hanafi school: something that is physically non-existent at the time of a contract may be deemed to exist in the eyes of the law for specific purposes. This principle is applied in salam (forward sale), where the goods being sold do not exist at the time of the contract but are considered existent in legal effect to permit the transaction. The maxim is an exception to the general rule that one cannot sell what one does not possess. It operates based on istihsan (juristic preference) and is limited to cases where the law expressly recognizes such treatment.

Key Terms
المعدوم (al-ma'dum): That which does not exist at the time of the contract, e.g., the goods in a salam contract.
حكمًا (hukman): In legal effect or by judicial estimation, as opposed to physical reality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Forward sale (salam)
C) Gift (hibah)
D) Mortgage (rahn)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'al-ma'dum yu'tabaru mawjudan hukman' is a controlled exception allowing salam and istisna' contracts to be valid despite the non-existence of the subject matter.

Valid Salam Requires Definable Description and Quantity
Explains the condition for salam validity with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for a valid salam contract and apply it to determine whether a specific object qualifies for salam.

Core Idea
A salam contract is valid only if the object's description and quantity are precisely definable.

This passage from al-Hidayah establishes a fundamental rule for salam (forward sale): the subject matter must be precisely describable and its quantity known. Without this condition, the contract leads to dispute and is invalid. The author illustrates with jewels (invalid) and bricks (valid). Understanding this condition is essential to ensure salam contracts are free from harmful uncertainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
Read the core statement: 'Whatever is possible to define its description and know its quantity, salam in it is valid.' This is the condition for validity.
The condition is two-fold: description (sifah) and quantity (qadr).
2
Understand the rationale (illah)
The text states that the condition prevents dispute (munaza'ah). This is the underlying cause for the rule—salam must avoid gharar.
The illah is the avoidance of future conflict.
3
Apply to examples
Jewels: variation makes description and quantity impossible → invalid. Bricks: if mold and size are specified, they become numerically similar → valid.
The key is whether the object can be standardised.
4
Recognise the textual evidence
The passage quotes al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and al-Jawhara al-Nayyira to show this is a established Zahir al-Riwayah ruling.
This ruling is relied upon (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school.
