Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling for the validity of a salam (forward sale) contract. The author establishes a fundamental condition: the object of sale must be precisely describable (ضبط صفته) and its quantity knowable (معرفة قدره). If these are possible, the contract is valid because it prevents future dispute (لا يفضي إلى المنازعة). If not, the contract is invalid because it leads to uncertainty and conflict. He then applies this principle to two examples: jewels (جواہرات) are not valid for salam because their variation makes precise description and quantity determination impossible. Bricks (اینٹ), on the other hand, are valid for salam if their mold (سانچہ), size, and quantity are specified, as they then become numerically similar (عددی متقارب). The passage then quotes the same principle from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and al-Jawhara al-Nayyira, showing that this is a well-established rule from the Zahir al-Riwayah. The key point is that the rationale (illah) for validity is the absence of dispute, which is a rational cause based on the prohibition of gharar (uncertainty) in sales.

Key Terms
سلم (salam): A forward sale where payment is made in advance for delivery of a specified commodity at a future date.
ضبط صفته (dabt sifatihi): Defining the description of the object in a manner that removes uncertainty.
منازعة (munaza'ah): Dispute or conflict that may arise from ambiguity in the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A salam contract is valid only if the subject matter can be described precisely and its quantity known, thereby avoiding dispute.

The Non-Existent as Legally Existent: The Principle Behind Istisna'
Explains the maxim and justifies istisna' by istihsan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a non-existent object can be considered legally existent to justify the validity of istisna' contracts and distinguish it from the qiyas ruling.

Core Idea
Istisna' is valid by istihsan because custom treats the non-existent item as legally existent, overriding qiyas.

The Hanafi school recognizes that certain transactions, while seemingly invalid by strict analogy (qiyas), are upheld due to established custom and necessity. One such case is istisna' (commissioning manufacture), where the object of sale does not exist at the time of contract. This lesson unpacks the maxim 'al-ma'dumu qad yu'tabaru mawjudan hukman' and shows how it resolves the apparent conflict between qiyas and istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is istisna'? It is a contract to commission the manufacture of a specific item. According to the Hanafi school, it is sahih (valid) by istihsan, despite the object being non-existent at contract.
The ruling is sahih, not batil as qiyas would suggest.
2
Examine the qiyas argument
By qiyas on the general rule that a sale of a non-existent object is batil due to gharar (uncertainty), istisna' would be invalid. Identify the illa (effective cause): non-existence of the object of sale.
The qiyas is straightforward: the object is ma'dum, so the sale is batil.
3
Introduce istihsan and its basis
The Hanafi school sets aside qiyas and rules by istihsan (juristic preference) because of the continuous custom (ta'amul) of the Muslim community. Custom acts as a consensus (ijma') that overrides qiyas.
Istihsan is a recognized source in Hanafi usul, allowing departure from strict analogy.
4
Analyze the analogies supporting istihsan
The text provides two analogies where non-existent entities are legally treated as existent: (1) the forgetful slaughterer's tasmiya is considered existent, and (2) the mustahadah's purification is considered existent. By analogy, the commissioned item is legally existent.
These analogies show that the law already recognizes hukmi (legal) existence in other contexts.
5
Apply the principle to istisna' conditions
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that istisna' is valid without a fixed term (bighayr ajalin). The contract must involve items commonly commissioned by custom, like basins, ewers, or boots.
If a fixed term is stipulated, it may transform into salam, which has its own rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۳۷) الْمَعْدُومُ قَدْ يُعْتَبَرُ مَوْجُودًا حُكْمًا.
ترجمہ: معدوم کو بھی حکما موجود مان لیا جاتا ہے۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ کبھی معدوم چیز کو حکما موجود مان لیا جاتا ہے، یعنی اگر چہ حقیقت دو چیز موجود نہیں ہے مگر حکما اسے موجود تصور کر لیا جاتا ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ استصناع عامتہ المشائخ کے یہاں بیج ہے اور استحسانا جائز ہے، استصناع کہتے ہیں کہ آرڈر پر کوئی چیز بنوانا، قیاس کا تقاضا یہ ہے کہ یہ جائز نہ ہو، اس لیے کہ اس میں مبیع معدوم ہوتی ہے اور معدوم شے کی بیع جائز نہیں ہے، لیکن تعامل امت کی وجہ سے یہ استحسانا جائز ہے، اس میں اگر چہ بیچ حقیقتا موجود نہیں ہوتی لیکن حکماً موجود ہوتی ہے، جیسے اگر مسلمان جانور ذبح کرتے ہوئے تسمیہ پڑھنا بھول جائے تو شرعاً اس کی معدوم تسمیہ کو حکماً موجود شمار کیا گیا ہے، اس طرح استصناع میں بھی معدوم چیز کو حکماً موجود کا درجہ دیا گیا ہے۔ 0
فَإِنْ قِيلَ: كَيْفَ يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَكُونَ بَيْعًا وَالْمَعْدُومُ لَا يَصْلُحُ أَنْ يَكُونَ مَبِيعًا . أَجَابَ بأَنَّ الْمَعْدُومَ قَدْ يُعْتَبَرُ مَوْجُودًا حُكْمًا) كَالنَّاسِي لِلتَّسْمِيَةِ عِنْدَ الذَّبْحِ، فَإِنَّ التَّسْمِيَةَ جُعِلَتْ مَوْجُودَةٌ لِعُذْرِ النِّسْيَانِ، وَالطَّهَارَةِ لِلْمُسْتَحَاضَةِ جُعِلَتْ مَوْجُودَةٌ لِعُذْرِ جَوَازِ الصَّلَوَاتِ لِئَلَّا تَتَضَاعَفَ الْوَاجِبَاتُ، فَكَذَلِكَ الْمُسْتَصْنَعُ الْمَعْدُومُ جُعِلَ مَوْجُودًا حُكْمًا لِلتَّعامُل. (العناية: كتاب البيوع، باب السلم، ج۷ ص ۱۱۵)
(وإن استصنع شيئا من ذلك أى من ما ذكر من الطست والقمقمة والخف (بغير أجل) أى بغير ذكر مدة فيه (جاز استحسانا للإجماع الثابت بالتعامل في هذه الأشياء ..... وفى القياس لا يجوز لأنه بيع المعدوم...... وجه الاستحسان هو ما ذكره بقوله: للإجماع الثابت بالتعامل، فإن الناس في سائر الأعصار تعارفوا الاستصناع فيما فيه تعامل من غير نكير والقياس يترك بمثله كدخول الحمام. (البناية كتاب البيوع، باب السلم، ج ۸ ص ۳۷۳، ۳۷۴)
(37) The non-existent can be considered existent in legal effect.
Translation: The non-existent is also accepted as existent legally.
Explanation: The meaning of the above principle is that sometimes a non-existent thing is considered existent legally, i.e., although in reality the thing does not exist, legally it is considered existent. The author of Hidayah, after mentioning this principle, is explaining that istisna' according to the majority of scholars (ammat al-masha'ikh) is a sale and is permissible by istihsan. Istisna' means commissioning someone to make something. The requirement of qiyas (analogy) is that it is not permissible, because in it the object of sale is non-existent, and the sale of a non-existent thing is not permissible. But due to the custom (ta'amul) of the ummah, it is permissible by istihsan. In it, although the sale item is not existent in reality, it is existent legally. Just as if a Muslim forgets to recite the tasmiya while slaughtering an animal, then legally his non-existent tasmiya is counted as existent. Similarly, in istisna', the non-existent thing is given the status of being existent legally. If it is said: How can it be a sale while the non-existent cannot be an object of sale? He answered: The non-existent can be considered existent legally, like the one who forgets the tasmiya during slaughter, for the tasmiya is considered existent due to the excuse of forgetfulness; and the purification for the mustahadah (chronically bleeding woman) is considered existent due to the excuse of allowing prayers lest the obligations multiply. Similarly, the commissioned non-existent item is made existent legally due to custom. (al-'Inaya, Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Salam, vol. 7 p. 115)
And if one commissions something from those items, i.e., from what was mentioned of basins, ewers, and boots, without an appointed term, i.e., without mentioning a time limit, it is permissible by istihsan due to the consensus established by custom in these things... And according to qiyas it is not permissible because it is a sale of a non-existent... The basis of istihsan is what he mentioned: consensus established by custom, for people in all eras have practiced istisna' in items of common dealing without disapproval, and qiyas is abandoned for something like this, just as entering the bathhouse (for a fee) is permitted. (al-Binaya, Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Salam, vol. 8 pp. 373-374)
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage introduces a key Hanafi usul (principle): al-ma'dumu qad yu'tabaru mawjudan hukman (the non-existent can be considered existent in legal effect). The author applies this principle to justify the validity of istisna' (commissioning manufacture) contracts, which would otherwise be invalid by qiyas because the object of sale is non-existent at the time of contract. The Hanafi school, following the majority of the mashayikh, rules istisna' as valid by istihsan due to the continuous practice (ta'amul) of the Muslim community across generations. The text provides two analogies to illustrate the principle: (1) the forgetful slaughterer: if one forgets to recite tasmiya, the non-existent tasmiya is legally considered existent, so the meat is halal. (2) the mustahadah (woman with chronic bleeding): her impurity is legally considered purified for the sake of allowing consecutive prayers so that obligations do not double. Thus, by analogy, the non-existent commissioned item is considered legally existent due to custom. The passage then quotes al-'Inaya and al-Binaya to support that istisna' is valid without a fixed term (ghayr ajalin) by istihsan, and that qiyas is set aside because of the established custom (ijma' bil ta'amul). The reference to entering the bathhouse (dukhul al-hammam) is another example where custom allows a contract that would otherwise be problematic by strict qiyas. This lesson shows that Hanafi fiqh utilizes istihsan as a source that can override qiyas when custom and necessity demand it.

Key Terms
معدوم (ma'dum): Something that does not exist at the time of the contract; a non-existent object.
استصناع (istisna'): A contract to commission the manufacture of a specific item, such as a basin, ewer, or boot.
استحسان (istihsan): A method of legal reasoning that prefers a more equitable outcome over strict qiyas, often based on custom or necessity.
تعامل (ta'amul): Widespread practice or custom of the community, considered a basis for consensus (ijma') in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the Hanafi ruling that istisna' (commissioning manufacture) is valid despite the object being non-existent at the time of contract?
A) Qiyas on the sale of existing goods.
B) A specific verse from the Quran.
C) Istihsan based on the continuous custom of the ummah.
D) The majority opinion of the Companions.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'al-ma'dumu qad yu'tabaru mawjudan hukman' allows istisna' contracts to be valid by istihsan, recognizing that custom can give non-existent objects legal existence.

Istisna' Valid by Istihsan: Treating Non-Existent as Existent Hukman
Explaining the legal fiction to justify istisna' by istihsan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why istisna' is valid in the Hanafi school despite the general rule against selling non-existent items, and apply the concept of legal fiction (hukman) to analogous cases.

Core Idea
Custom makes the non-existent legally existent, allowing istisna' to override the qiyas prohibition.

This lesson explores a central Hanafi argument for the permissibility of istisna' (manufacturing contract). The text presents an objection–response format: how can a sale be valid when the item is non-existent? The answer introduces the powerful tool of istihsan and the concept of treating the non-existent as existent hukman (in legal ruling), supported by analogies from tasmiyah at slaughter and the purity of the mustahadah. By the end, you will understand how custom (ta'amul) can establish a consensus that overrides qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector argues that istisna' cannot be a valid sale because the item does not exist at the time of contract. Understand the general prohibition: selling what one does not possess.
This qiyas-based objection is strong; the Hanafi response must provide a convincing counter.
2
Locate the Hanafi response: legal fiction
The response introduces the concept of considering the non-existent as existent hukman (in legal ruling). This is a legal fiction that has precedent in other areas of fiqh.
3
Analyze the analogies
The text gives two analogies: (1) forgetting tasmiyah at slaughter – the tasmiyah is considered existent due to forgetfulness; (2) the mustahadah – her purity is considered existent to allow continuous worship. Both show that necessity and custom can override the literal reality.
Note how these analogies share the feature of a missing element being legally supplied due to a valid excuse.
4
Understand the role of ta'amul
The ultimate justification is the continuous mutual practice (ta'amul) of people across ages. This widespread practice constitutes an implicit consensus (ijma' 'amali) that overrides qiyas. Qiyas is abandoned like in the case of entering a bathhouse.
This is a classic use of istihsan: departing from qiyas for a stronger evidence (here, custom).
5
Distinguish from other contracts
Note that istisna' differs from salam (advanced payment) because it does not require a fixed term. The legal fiction of considering the item existent is unique to istisna' and based on custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ قِيلَ: كَيْفَ يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَكُونَ بَيْعًا وَالْمَعْدُومُ لَا يَصْلُحُ أَنْ يَكُونَ مَبِيعًا . أَجَابَ بأَنَّ الْمَعْدُومَ قَدْ يُعْتَبَرُ مَوْجُودًا حُكْمًا) كَالنَّاسِي لِلتَّسْمِيَةِ عِنْدَ الذَّبْحِ، فَإِنَّ التَّسْمِيَةَ جُعِلَتْ مَوْجُودَةٌ لِعُذْرِ النِّسْيَانِ، وَالطَّهَارَةِ لِلْمُسْتَحَاضَةِ جُعِلَتْ مَوْجُودَةٌ لِعُذْرِ جَوَازِ الصَّلَوَاتِ لِئَلَّا تَتَضَاعَفَ الْوَاجِبَاتُ، فَكَذَلِكَ الْمُسْتَصْنَعُ الْمَعْدُومُ جُعِلَ مَوْجُودًا حُكْمًا لِلتَّعامُل. (العناية: كتاب البيوع، باب السلم، ج۷ ص ١١٥) (وإن استصنع شيئا من ذلك أى من ما ذكر من الطست والقمقمة والخف (بغير أجل) أى بغير ذكر مدة فيه (جاز استحسانا للإجماع الثابت بالتعامل في هذه الأشياء ..... وفى القياس لا يجوز لأنه بيع المعدوم...... وجه الاستحسان هو ما ذكره بقوله: للإجماع الثابت بالتعامل، فإن الناس في سائر الأعصار تعارفوا الاستصناع فيما فيه تعامل من غير نكير والقياس يترك بمثله كدخول الحمام. (البناية كتاب البيوع، باب السلم، ج ۸ ص ۳۷۳، ۳۷٤)
If it is said: How can it be a sale when a non-existent thing is unfit to be a sold object? He answered: Because the non-existent is sometimes considered existent in legal ruling, like the one who forgot the tasmiyah at the time of slaughter, for the tasmiyah is considered existent due to the excuse of forgetfulness, and the purity for the mustahadah is considered existent due to the excuse of permitting prayers so that obligations do not multiply. Likewise, the manufactured non-existent item is considered existent in legal ruling due to common practice. (Al-Inayah: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Salam, vol. 7 p. 115) And if one orders the manufacture of something from those items mentioned, like a basin, kettle, or boot, without a fixed term, it is permissible by istihsan due to consensus established by common practice regarding these things... In qiyas it is not permissible because it is a sale of a non-existent item... The basis of istihsan is what he mentioned: due to consensus established by common practice, for people in all ages have mutually practiced istisna' regarding things they deal in without disapproval, and qiyas is abandoned by something like this, like entering the bathhouse. (Al-Binayah: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Salam, vol. 8 pp. 373-374)
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the validity of istisna' (manufacturing contract) by istihsan. The text presents a Q&A format: an objector questions how istisna' can be a valid sale when the sold item is non-existent at the time of contract. The Hanafi response, drawn from Al-Inayah, explains that a non-existent thing can be legally treated as existent (hukman) based on necessity and custom. This legal fiction is supported by analogous cases: (1) forgetting tasmiyah at slaughter—the forgotten invocation is legally considered existent, making the meat halal; (2) the mustahadah (woman with chronic bleeding)—her invalid purity (istihalah) is legally considered existing to allow continuous prayers. Similarly, the non-existent item in istisna' is legally considered existent due to widespread practice (ta'amul). The second quote from Al-Binayah clarifies that istisna' without a fixed term is permissible by istihsan because of consensus derived from continuous custom. In qiyas (analogy to regular sale), it would be invalid because it sells a non-existent object, but istihsan overrides qiyas here, just as it overrides qiyas in the case of entering a bathhouse (where the service is non-existent at contract). Thus, the Hanafi school makes istisna' an exception to the general rule against selling non-existent items.

Key Terms
استصناع (istisna'): A contract to manufacture a specific item (e.g., basin, boot) according to order, without a fixed term or delivery date.
حكمًا (hukman): Legally, in terms of legal ruling, as opposed to physically.
استحسان (istihsan): A departure from qiyas based on a stronger evidence or custom.
تعامل (ta'amul): Mutual practice among people that has become widespread and known.

D) Because qiyas permits it as an exception.

Key Takeaway
Istisna' is permissible by istihsan because the non-existent is legally considered existent hukman due to continuous custom, providing a practical exception to the prohibition of selling absent items.

Increase in Price or Object: Permissible and Attaches to Original Contract
Stating the ruling that adding to price or object in a sale is permissible and attaches to the original contract.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on increase in price or object in a sale, explain the underlying istihsan reasoning, and apply the ruling to a similar scenario.

Core Idea
Increase in price or object is permissible by istihsan and attaches to the original contract.

In Hanafi fiqh, modifications to a sale contract after the initial agreement are handled with flexibility. This lesson examines the ruling that adding to the price (thaman) or the object (muthman) is permissible and becomes part of the original contract, rather than requiring a new one. The key legal tool here is istihsan (juristic preference), which overrides strict analogy to facilitate transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what exactly is being ruled upon: an increase in the price or the object after the initial agreement. The ruling is 'jai'iz' (permissible) according to the Hanafi school.
The increase can come from the buyer, seller, or a third party.
2
Locate the dalil or basis
The text states the ruling is 'by istihsan'. Istihsan is a method of departing from qiyas (analogy) in favor of convenience, custom, or necessity. The proof here is that the increase reveals that the contract was actually concluded on the total amount including the increase.
Strict qiyas might require a new contract, but istihsan permits attachment.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how istihsan applies: by the increase, it becomes clear that the contract was intended to cover both the original and the increased amount together. The increase is not a separate transaction but part of the same deal.
The example of a third party guaranteeing extra payment illustrates this.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other madhhabs may require a separate new contract for any increase, sticking to strict qiyas. The Hanafi school is known for using istihsan in such cases.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different weights given to qiyas vs. istihsan.
5
Apply the ruling to the example
In the example, a buyer wants to buy a slave for 1000 dirhams, the seller demands 1500. A third party offers to guarantee the extra 500. The sale is valid for 1500 total, with the third party's guarantee attaching to the original contract. The increase (via guarantee) is part of the original sale.
The third party becomes liable for 500; the buyer pays 1000. The seller receives 1500 total.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الزيادة على الثمن والمثمن جائزة عندنا . ونظيره ما قلنا في الزيادة فى الثمن والمثمن أنها جائزة استحسانا وتلتحق بأصل العقد لأن بالزيادة تبين أن العقد وقع على المزيد عليه وعلى الزيادة جميعا. (بدائع الصنائع: كتاب البيوع، فصل في شرائط البيع، ج ۵ ص ٢٠٦) (قوله: ومن قال لغيره بع عبدك من فلان صورته أن يطلب إنسان من آخر شراء عبده بألف درهم وهو لا يبيع إلا بألف وخمسمائة والمشتري لا يرغب فيه إلا بألف فيجيء آخر ويقول لصاحب العبد بع عبدك هذا من هذا الرجل بألف على أنى ضامن لك خمسمائة من الثمن سوى الألف فهو جائز ويأخذ
Increase in the price and the priced object is permissible according to us. Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof concerning increase in the price (thaman) or the object (muthman) in a sale. The Hanafi position is that such an increase is permissible and it becomes attached to the original contract. The proof is that the increase reveals that the contract was actually concluded on the total amount including the increase. The text gives an example: a buyer wants to buy a slave for 1000 dirhams, but the seller demands 1500. A third party intervenes, telling the seller to sell for 1000, and offering to be a guarantor for the remaining 500. This is valid because the third party's guarantee effectively increases the price. The increase (the 500) attaches to the original contract, so the total price becomes 1500, with the buyer paying 1000 and the third party paying 500. The ruling is based on istihsan, meaning it departs from strict analogy (which might require a new contract) in favor of custom and facilitation.

Key Terms

ثمن (thaman): Price, consideration paid by the buyer.
مثمن (muthman): The priced object, the good being sold.
التحق (iltiḥaq): To attach or annex to the original contract.

Permissibility of Increasing Price or Object in a Sale (Istihsan)
Explaining the principle that increasing price or object in sale is valid by istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on increasing price or object in a sale, explain the basis of istihsan, and apply it to the scenario of a third-party guarantor.

Core Idea
Increasing price or object in a sale is valid; the addition attaches to the original contract by istihsan.

In this lesson, we examine a key Hanafi principle from Al-Hidayah: it is permissible to increase the price (thaman) or the object of sale (muthamman) after the contract is initiated. This ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference), which allows flexibility in transactions. The passage illustrates this with a scenario where a third party guarantees the difference between the seller's asking price and the buyer's offer. Understanding this principle clarifies how Hanafi law accommodates practical needs in commercial dealings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (increasing price or object in a sale) and the ruling (valid by istihsan).
The ruling is sahih (valid), not batil or fasid.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is istihsan, a departure from strict qiyas for public good.
Strict qiyas would not allow post-contract increases.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how istihsan justifies the ruling: the increase shows the contract was intended for the total amount from the beginning.
The addition is annexed to the original contract.
4
Apply to the example
Work through the scenario: seller wants 1500, buyer offers 1000, third party guarantees 500. The sale is valid, and the third party must pay the 500.
If the guarantor does not say 'from the price', no guarantee.

(العناية: كتاب البيوع، باب السلم مسائل منثورة، ج۷ ص ۱۲۳،۱۲۲)
Usool E Hidayah Volume 3 - Scattered Issues. (38) Increasing the price and the object of sale is permissible according to us. Translation: According to us, increasing the price and the object is permissible. 73 Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that according to the Hanafis, it is permissible to add to the price and the sold item in a sale, and this addition must be given, whether the addition is made by one of the contracting parties or by a third party. The author of Hidayah, after mentioning the principle, explains that if a seller wants to sell an item for one thousand five hundred (1500) dirhams and the buyer wants to buy it for one thousand (1000), then a third person says to the seller: 'Take the one thousand dirhams from the buyer, and I guarantee the remaining five hundred dirhams,' then this sale is valid, because according to us, it is valid to increase the price and the object, and this addition will attach to the original contract, and it will be said that the contract occurred on that basis. Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Salam, Masa'il Manthura, vol. 3, p. 08: 'And its parallel is what we said regarding the increase in the price and the object, that it is permissible by istihsan, and it attaches to the original contract, because by the increase it becomes clear that the contract occurred on the increased amount and the increase together.' (Badai' al-Sanai': Kitab al-Buyu', Fasl fi Shara'it al-Bay', vol. 5, p. 206) (His saying: 'And whoever says to another: Sell your slave to so-and-so' - its form is that a person asks another to buy his slave for one thousand dirhams, but he will not sell except for one thousand five hundred, and the buyer does not want it except for one thousand. Then another person comes and says to the owner of the slave: 'Sell this slave of yours to this man for one thousand on condition that I am the guarantor for you of five hundred from the price besides the one thousand.' This is permissible, and he takes the one thousand from the buyer and the five hundred from the guarantor. And if he does not say 'from the price', the sale is valid for one thousand and nothing is upon the guarantor. The difference between them is what he mentioned based on the previous principle (that increasing in the price and the object is permissible according to us and attaches to the original contract). (Al-'Inayah: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Salam, Masa'il Manthura, vol. 7, pp. 122-123)
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (hukm ma'a al-dalil) from the Hanafi school. It states the principle that in a sale, adding to the price (thaman) or the object of sale (muthamman) is valid and the addition is considered part of the original contract. The author uses an example: a seller wants 1500 dirhams, a buyer offers 1000, and a third party guarantees the additional 500. The sale is valid, and the third party's guarantee is binding. The proof is istihsan (juristic preference) as stated in the text: the addition clarifies that the contract was intended for the total amount from the beginning. The passage also discusses a nuance: if the guarantor says 'I guarantee you five hundred' without specifying 'from the price,' then no guarantee is owed. This difference is based on the principle. The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah, as it appears in Al-Hidayah and other core Hanafi works.

Key Terms
ثمن (thaman): The price in a sale transaction.
مثمن (muthamman): The object of sale.
زیادہ (ziyadah): An addition to the price or object after the contract is formed.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee; the third party guarantees the additional amount.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a seller wants 1500 dirhams and a buyer offers 1000, and a third party guarantees the additional 500, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) The sale is invalid because the price is not fixed.
B) The sale is valid only if the third party is a relative.
C) The sale is valid and the third party must pay the 500.
D) The sale is valid only if the third party pays the 500 immediately.

Key Takeaway
Increasing the price or object in a sale is valid and attaches to the original contract by istihsan.

Absolute Exchange in Sarf: The Hanafi Principle of Individual Pairing
Statement of a Hanafi principle in Sarf with inter-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle of absolute exchange in Sarf, apply it to mixed currency cases, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i/Zafar view.

Core Idea
An absolute exchange can be validly split into individual cross-currency pairs, avoiding riba.

When you exchange a bundle of coins — say two dirhams and one dinar for two dinars and one dirham — does the contract hold? The Hanafi school says yes, based on a powerful interpretive principle: absolute exchange implies individual-for-individual exchange. This lesson unpacks that principle and the dispute it generates with other schools.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school holds regarding the validity of an absolute exchange in Sarf. The contract is sahih (valid) because the ambiguity can be resolved by interpreting each item as being exchanged for its counterpart across currency types.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi madhhab.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Zafar hold the opposite view: the contract is invalid due to ambiguity and potential riba.
They require explicit pairing to avoid uncertainty.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine why Shafi'i and Zafar reject the contract: the surplus in the same currency (e.g., two dirhams for one dirham) could lead to riba al-fadl if not properly accounted for.
Their concern is the possibility of riba when the exchange is not clearly split.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal: the absolute contract can be validly interpreted as two separate cross-currency exchanges (each dirham for a dinar), making any surplus lawful because it is across different currency types.
The principle 'al-muqābalah al-muṭlaqah taḥtamil muqābalah al-fard bil-fard' is the key.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: Hanafis allow such mixed exchanges; Shafi'is and Zafar require specification. This affects everyday trade where currency bundles are exchanged.
The ruling difference directly impacts contract validity in Sarf transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْمُقَابَلَةُ الْمُطْلَقَةُ تَحْتَمِلُ مُقَابَلَةَ الْفَرْدِ بِالْفَرْدِ.
Absolute exchange implies the exchange of individual for individual.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage introduces a legal principle in the chapter of Sarf (currency exchange). The principle: when two parties exchange a mixture of items (e.g., two dirhams and one dinar for two dinars and one dirham) without specifying how each item is matched, the exchange is valid. The Hanafi interpretation is that the contract can be understood as a set of individual exchanges: each dirham is exchanged for a dinar individually, and the surplus in each currency that would normally be usurious is allowed because it is cross-currency. For example, two dirhams plus one dinar for two dinars plus one dirham is interpreted as two dirhams for two dinars (cross-currency, no problem) and one dinar for one dirham (also cross-currency). Imam Shafi'i and Imam Zafar disagree, holding that the exchange is invalid due to ambiguity. The Hanafi position relies on this principle: absolute exchange (mubhama) can be given a valid interpretation that makes the contract sound. The principle is recorded in Al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
مقابلة مطلقة (muqābalah muṭlaqah): An exchange where the items are not explicitly paired; the contract is phrased as a lump sum of items for another lump sum.
فرد بالفرد (fard bil-fard): Individual for individual: each unit of one currency is exchanged for a unit of the other currency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle 'absolute exchange implies individual-to-individual exchange', what happens when someone gives 2 dirhams and 1 dinar for 2 dinars and 1 dirham?
A) The contract is invalid due to ambiguity
B) The contract is valid and each item is exchanged for its counterpart
C) The contract is valid only if both parties consent to the interpretation
D) The contract is valid but the surplus in dirhams is considered riba

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle allows absolute exchanges in Sarf to be interpreted as individual cross-currency trades, validating mixed currency contracts that would otherwise be considered ambiguous.

Principle of Absolute Matching in Exchange Contracts
States and explains a Hanafi principle about matching opposite types in a single transaction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on selling mixed ribawi items and evaluate the opposing Shafi'i/Zufar view by applying the principle of absolute matching.

Core Idea
Absolute matching allows interpreting mixed currency sales as valid by matching individual items across types.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi principle that resolves potential riba in mixed currency transactions. By analyzing a specific case where dirhams and dinars are exchanged in a bundle, we see how the Hanafi school uses the ambiguity of absolute matching to validate the contract. This contrasts with the stricter view of Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Zufar.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the sale of two dirhams and one dinar for two dinars and one dirham is valid according to the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the opposing view
State that Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Zufar consider such a transaction invalid due to potential riba.
This is an inter-madhhab dispute.
3
Follow the Hanafi proof using the principle
Explain how the principle of absolute matching (muqābalah muṭlaqah) allows interpreting the transaction as individual-to-individual matching across opposite types, making any excess permissible.
The key is that riba only applies within the same type.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the opposing argument
The Hanafis prioritize the interpretation that saves the contract, arguing that absolute matching is ambiguous and the valid interpretation is chosen.
This is based on the legal preference for validity.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis allow mixed currency transactions; Shafi'is and Zufar prohibit them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۴۰) الْمُقَابَلَةُ الْمُطْلَقَةُ تَحْتَمِلُ مُقَابَلَةَ الْفَرْدِ بِالْفَرْدِ.
ترجمہ: مقابلہ مطلقہ مقابلہ فرد بالفرد کا احتمال رکھتا ہے۔ تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جب بائع مشتری بیع میں مطلق تقابل کریں، تو اس میں یہ بھی احتمال ہے کہ مجموعہ کا مجموعے کے ساتھ تقابل ہو، اسی طرح یہ بھی احتمال ہوتا ہے کہ فرد کا فرد کے ساتھ تقابل ہو۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ اقتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر کسی نے دو درہم اور ایک دینار کو دو دینار اور ایک درہم کے عوض فروخت کر دیا تو یہ بیج جائز ہے، اور عوضین میں سے ہر ایک عوض کو خلاف جنس کی طرف پھیرا جائے گا، کیونکہ مذکورہ صورت میں بائع مشتری نے مطلق تقابل کیا ہے اور مطلق تقابل فرد کو فرد کے تقابل کا بھی احتمال رکھتا ہے اور مجموعے کو مجموعے کے ساتھ تقابل کا بھی احتمال رکھتا ہے، اب یہاں فرد کا فرد کے ساتھ خلاف جنس میں تقابل ہو گا ، اب دو درہم دو دینار کے عوض اور ایک دینار ایک درہم کے عوض ہو جائے گا، اور چونکہ خلاف جنس کی صورت میں زیادتی جائز ہوتی ہے، لہذا مذکورہ صورت میں بھی زیادتی جائز ہوگی اور یہ بیچ درست ہوگی ، امام شافعی اور امام زفر رحمہما اللہ کے نزدیک یہ بیچ جائز نہیں ہے، جبکہ احناف کے ہاں یہ مطلق عقد ہے، لہذا اعوضین میں سے ہر ایک کو اس کے خلاف جنس کی طرف پھریں گے یعنی دو دینار کو دو درہم کے عوض اور ایک دینار کو ایک درہم کے عوض ، تو اب اس میں کمی زیادتی درست ہوگی ،صورت مسئلہ میں جو دو درہم اور ایک دینار کا دو دینار اور ایک درہم کے ساتھ مقابلہ کیا گیا ہے وہ مقابلہ مطلق ہے، اس میں یہ بھی احتمال ہے کہ مجموعے کو مجموعے کے ساتھ تقابل کریں اور یہ بھی احتمال ہے کہ فرد کو فرد کے ساتھ تقابل کریں، اب احناف نے فرد کا فرد کے ساتھ خلاف جنس میں تقابل کیا تاکہ عاقدین کا تصرف درست ہو جائے ، تو گویا یہاں مقابلہ مطلقہ سے مراد مقابلہ فرد بالفرد متعین ہے اور دیگر احتمال باطل ہیں ۔
(40) Absolute matching may imply matching of individual with individual. Translation: Absolute matching carries the possibility of matching individual with individual. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that when seller and buyer engage in absolute matching in a sale, it is possible that the total is matched with the total, and it is also possible that individual is matched with individual. The author of al-Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, intends to say that if someone sells two dirhams and one dinar in exchange for two dinars and one dirham, this sale is valid, and each of the two countervalues will be diverted to its opposite kind, because in this case the seller and buyer have made an absolute matching, and absolute matching carries the possibility of individual-to-individual matching as well as total-to-total matching. Here, individual-to-individual matching will occur across opposite types, so two dirhams will be in exchange for two dinars, and one dinar for one dirham. Since excess is permissible across different types, therefore in this case the excess is permissible and the sale is valid. According to Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Zufar (may Allah have mercy on them), this sale is not valid, whereas according to the Hanafis it is an absolute contract, so each of the two countervalues will be turned to its opposite type, i.e., two dinars for two dirhams and one dinar for one dirham, so then any increment or decrement is permissible. In the case at hand, the matching of two dirhams and one dinar with two dinars and one dirham is absolute matching; it is possible to match total with total and also individual with individual. The Hanafis have matched individual with individual across opposite types so that the transaction of the parties becomes valid. So it is as if here the absolute matching means that matching individual with individual is determined and the other possibilities are invalid.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning a transaction where two dirhams and one dinar are sold for two dinars and one dirham. The Hanafi position, supported by the principle 'absolute matching may imply individual-to-individual matching,' holds the sale valid. The author explains that when the matching is absolute (unconditional), it can be interpreted either as a total-for-total exchange or as an individual-for-individual exchange. The Hanafis choose the latter interpretation across opposite types (e.g., dirhams against dinars) to validate the contract, since excess (fadl) is permissible in ribawi goods of different types. Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Zufar (the student of Abu Hanifa) reject this, considering such a transaction invalid due to potential riba. The passage thus illustrates a distinctive Hanafi ijtihad: prioritizing the validity of the parties' transaction by interpreting ambiguous contracts in a manner that avoids prohibition. The key legal tool here is the principle (asl) that absolute matching carries multiple probabilities, and the Hanafi school opts for the interpretation that saves the contract.

Key Terms
مقابلة مطلقة (muqābalah muṭlaqah): Absolute matching: an unconditional pairing of two sets of items in a contract without specifying which item corresponds to which.
خلاف الجنس (khilāf al-jins): Opposite type: goods of different categories (e.g., dirhams vs. dinars) where excess is permissible in exchange.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) By matching total to total, thus treating it as a single exchange of two dirhams for two dinars and one dinar for one dirham as separate contracts.
B) By matching each individual item to its opposite type, so that two dirhams correspond to two dinars and one dinar to one dirham, allowing any excess.
C) By cancelling the contract because the items are not equivalent.
D) By requiring both parties to agree on a single price for the whole bundle.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of absolute matching allows mixed ribawi transactions to be valid by interpreting individual-to-individual matching across opposite types.

Rescission by Necessitation: Implicit Annulment in Mixed Ribawi Sales
Explains the principle of rescission by necessitation through examples of mixed ribawi exchanges.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle of rescission by necessitation and apply it to mixed ribawi exchanges to validate the contract.

Core Idea
A contract can be implicitly rescinded to validate a subsequent contract—necessity overcomes explicit formality.

In Hanafi fiqh, not all rescissions require explicit verbal annulment. Sometimes the law infers a rescission out of necessity (iqtiḍā') to save a transaction from invalidity. This lesson explores that principle through the classic example of mixed currency and goods exchanges, where matching each type to its opposite avoids riba but requires implied rescission of the original matching.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem
Consider a sale of two dirhams and one dinar for one dirham and two dinars. This involves ribawi goods of different types (currency). If each type were matched to its own (dirham for dirham, dinar for dinar), one side would receive more of one type, potentially involving riba.
The problem arises because the quantities are unequal within each type.
2
State the Hanafi Solution
The Hanafi rule is to match each type to its opposite: the two dirhams are exchanged for the two dinars, and the one dinar is exchanged for the one dirham, thereby equalizing within each type (1 dirham for 1 dirham, 1 dinar for 1 dinar) and avoiding riba.
This requires treating the contract as two separate exchanges.
3
Explain the Necessitation (Iqtiḍā')
For the matching to opposite types to be valid, the original implication that each type matches its own must be rescinded. This rescission is not explicit but is necessitated by the need to validate the contract. The principle is that rescission can be established by iqtiḍā' when required to avoid invalidity.
This is the key innovative principle of the lesson.
4
Note the Opposing View
Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that such a sale is invalid because they require explicit rescission or do not accept the implicit re-matching. The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) prevails in the school.
The dispute highlights the methodological difference in handling contractual interpretation.
5
Apply to the Examples
The same principle applies to agricultural ribawi goods: selling one kor of wheat and one kor of barley for two kor of wheat and two kor of barley. The sale is validated by matching one kor of wheat to one kor of barley, and the other kor of barley to the other kor of wheat, rescinding the original matching implicitly.
Extension of the principle shows its consistent application across ribawi goods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۴۱) الْفَسُحُ قَدْ يَثْبُتُ بِطَرِيقِ الْاِقْتِضَاءِ.
ترجمہ: فسخ کبھی بطریق اقتضاء ثابت ہوتا ہے۔ تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ کبھی عقد اقتضاء النص کے طریق پر ضمنا فخ ہو جاتا ہے صراحنافخ کی ضرورت نہیں ہوتی ، دوسرے عقد کی درستگی کے لیے پہلے عقد کا فنح ضروری ہوتا ہے، اس لیے اس کا صیح ہونا اقتضاء ثابت ہوتا ہے۔ (وَمَنْ بَاعَ دِرُهَمَيْنِ وَدِيـنَـارًا بِدِرْهَمٍ وَدِينَارَيْنِ جَازَ الْبَيْعُ إِلَخَ) رَجُلٌ بَاعَ دِرْهَمَيْنِ وَدِينَارًا بِدِرْهَمٍ وَدِينَارَيْنِ جَازَ الْبَيْعُ وَجُعِلَ كُلُّ جِنْسِ بِخِلَافِهِ، وَقَالَ زُفَرُ وَالشَّافِعِيُّ رَحِمَهُمَا اللَّهُ: لَا يَجُوزُ، وَعَلَى هَذَا إِذَا بَاعَ كُرَّ شَعِيرٍ وَكُرَّ حِنْطَةٍ بِكُرَى شَعِيرٍ وَكُرَّى حِنْطَةٍ، وَالأصْلُ أَنَّ الْأَمْوَالَ الرِّبَوِيَّةَ الْمُخْتَلِفَةَ الْجِنْسِ إِذَا اشْتَمَلَ عَلَيْهَا الصَّفَقَةُ، وَكَانَ فِي صَرْفِ الْجِنْسِ إِلَى الْجِنْسِ فَسَادُ الْمُبَادَلَةِ يَصْرِف كُلُّ جِنْسِ مِنْهَا إِلَى خِلَافِ جِنْسِهَا عِندَ الْعُلَمَاءِ الثَّلَاثَةِ تَصْحِيحًا لِلْعَقْدِ. (العناية: كتاب الصرف، ج ۷ ص ۱۴۴) (قال) أي القدوري رحمه الله ومن باع در همین و دينارا بدرهم ودينارين جاز البيع وجعل كل جنس منهما بخلافه) تصحيحا للعقد، وكذا على هذا إذا باع كر حنطة وكر شعير بكرى حنطة وكرى شعير. (البناية كتاب الصرف، ج۸ ص ۴۰۳) الهداية كتاب البيوع، كتاب الصرف، ج ۳ ص ۱۱۵
(41) Rescission may be established through necessitation. Translation: Rescission is sometimes established by way of implication. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that sometimes a contract is implicitly rescinded by way of necessary inference from the text (iqtiḍā' al-naṣṣ) without the need for explicit rescission; for the validity of another contract, the rescission of the first contract is necessary, so its validity is established by implication. (And whoever sold two dirhams and a dinar for a dirham and two dinars, the sale is valid, etc.) A man sold two dirhams and a dinar for a dirham and two dinars; the sale is valid and each type is made [to correspond] to its opposite. Zufar and al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on them) said: it is not valid. And similarly if one sold a kor of barley and a kor of wheat for two kor of barley and two kor of wheat. The principle is that when ribawi goods of different types are included in a single transaction, and if matching type to type would spoil the exchange, each type is diverted to its opposite according to the three scholars to validate the contract. (al-Ināyah: Kitāb al-Ṣarf, vol. 7, p. 144) (He said i.e. al-Qudūrī), and whoever sold two dirhams and a dinar for a dirham and two dinars, the sale is valid and each of the two types is made to its opposite to validate the contract. Similarly, if one sold a kor of wheat and a kor of barley for two kor of wheat and two kor of barley. (al-Bināyah: Kitāb al-Ṣarf, vol. 8, p. 403) al-Hidāyah: Kitāb al-Buyū‘, Kitāb al-Ṣarf, vol. 3, p. 115.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage introduces the principle that rescission (faskh) of a contract can be established by way of necessitation (iqtiḍā'). This means that sometimes, to validate a subsequent contract, the previous contract is implicitly rescinded without express verbal rescission. The example given is the same mixed exchange as in the previous lesson: selling two dirhams and one dinar for one dirham and two dinars. The rule is applied that to validate the second contract, the first contract must be considered implicitly rescinded. The text also extends the principle to agricultural goods: selling one kor of barley and one kor of wheat for two kor of barley and two kor of wheat. In all these cases, the Hanafi school (and other scholars as mentioned) treat the exchange as valid by matching each type to its opposite, thereby implicitly rescinding the original matching that would have caused invalidity. The principle thus serves to validate contracts that might otherwise be deemed void due to riba concerns.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or annulment of a contract.
اقتضاء (iqtiḍā'): Necessitation/implication: a legal inference that something must be true for another ruling to be valid.
ربوي (ribawī): Riba-able goods: commodities subject to the prohibition of riba when exchanged in certain ways.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi principle of rescission by necessitation, what is the primary reason for implicitly rescinding a previous contract?
A) To allow the parties to change their minds without penalty.
B) To validate a subsequent contract that would otherwise be invalid due to riba.
C) To satisfy the requirement of explicit verbal rescission in all cases.
D) To ensure that each party gets exactly the same items they originally had.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, rescission (faskh) can be established by way of necessitation (iqtiḍā') to validate contracts involving mixed ribawi goods of different types.

Witnesses Are for Proof: The Principle of Evidence in Disputes
States a legal maxim and applies it to conflicting testimonies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-bayyinat li al-ithbat' to resolve conflicting witness testimonies in sale disputes and explain why the witness proving the increase is preferred according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Evidence is to prove, not to negate; the witness proving more is stronger.

In legal disputes, when both parties bring witnesses, how does a judge decide whose testimony to accept? The Hanafi school, as explained in Al-Hidayah, applies a simple but powerful maxim: witnesses are for proof. This lesson unpacks that maxim and shows how it resolves conflicting testimonies by favoring the evidence that proves the greater amount, whether in price or quantity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its core meaning
Find the key statement 'al-bayyinat li al-ithbat' and understand that witnesses are meant to establish a positive claim, not to negate. This sets the purpose of evidence.
2
Apply the maxim to a sale dispute
Consider a scenario where the seller and buyer disagree on the price or quantity of the sold item, and both produce witnesses. Determine which party's witnesses are proving an increase (more) over the other's claim.
Example: seller claims 2000, buyer claims 1000 – seller's witnesses prove increase; seller claims 5kg, buyer claims 10kg – buyer's witnesses prove increase.
3
Conclude which witnesses are preferred
State that the witnesses who prove the increase are accepted because they fulfill the purpose of evidence (ithbat) to a greater degree. The other witnesses are not rejected as false but simply outweighed.
This is because evidence is for proof, and the stronger proof prevails.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i view
Note that Imam al-Shafi'i holds that when both parties bring witnesses, the testimonies cancel out (ta'adul al-bayyinat) and the case is decided by oath. Understand the practical difference: Hanafi decides by evidence, Shafi'i by oath.
This contrast highlights the Hanafi emphasis on the function of evidence.
5
Understand the rationale (qiyas)
The ruling is based on qiyas: evidence is designed for proof, so whatever proves more is stronger. This is a rational inference from the purpose of evidence, consistent with Hanafi usul.
The reasoning is that if witnesses are for proof, then the one that proves more is more effective in that purpose.

Witnesses are for proof. Translation: Witnesses are for establishing proof. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that witnesses are for establishing something, not for negation. Therefore, whose witnesses prove an increase, they will be considered credible. The author of Al-Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, is explaining that if the seller and buyer differ over the quantity of the correct price, for example, the buyer says the price is 1000 rupees and the seller says 2000, similarly the seller claims the sold item is 5 kg of wheat and the buyer claims it is 10 kg, and both produce their witnesses, then the witnesses who prove the increase will be accepted, because witnesses are designed for proof. Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the legal maxim 'al-bayyinat li al-ithbat' (witnesses are for proof). The commentator explains that witnesses are meant to establish a positive claim, not to negate. When two parties dispute over an amount or quantity, the one whose witnesses prove an increase (i.e., a greater amount) is preferred. This is because the function of evidence is to prove, and the more it proves, the stronger it is. The example given is from a sale dispute: if the buyer and seller disagree on the price or the quantity of the commodity, and both bring witnesses, then the witnesses that prove the increase are accepted. For instance, if the seller claims the price is 2000 and the buyer says 1000, the seller's witnesses prove a greater amount, so they are preferred. Conversely, if the seller claims the commodity is 5 kg and the buyer says 10 kg, the buyer's witnesses prove a greater quantity, so they are preferred. The passage also quotes from Al-'Inayah and Al-Lubab to support related rulings, such as granting a plaintiff a guarantor for the defendant to ensure he does not flee, which is permissible by istihsan because it benefits the plaintiff with minimal harm to the defendant. This illustrates the Hanafi approach of balancing justice and practicality.

Key Terms
الْبَيِّنَاتُ (al-bayyināt): Formal legal evidence, specifically witness testimony in court.
الإِثْبَاتِ (al-ithbāt): Proof or establishment of a claim, as opposed to negation (nafy).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The buyer's witnesses, because they prove a lesser amount.
B) The seller's witnesses, because they prove the increase.
C) Neither, because they contradict each other.
D) The judge decides by lot.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'al-bayyinat li al-ithbat' resolves conflicting testimonies by preferring the evidence that proves the greater amount, as it fulfills the purpose of evidence.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Mutual Oath Depends on Dispute over Subject Matter and Price
Defines the scope for applying mutual oaths in sale disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the specific conditions under which mutual oath (tahlif) applies in a sale dispute and distinguish cases where it does not.

Core Idea
Mutual oath only applies when seller and buyer disagree on the item or price — not on payment terms or conditions.

This passage from Al-Hidayah establishes a key procedural rule in sale disputes: the mutual oath procedure (tahlif) is reserved for fundamental disagreements about the very object of the sale or its price. If the parties disagree on ancillary matters like the payment term or a condition of option, the ordinary rule of 'the denier takes an oath' applies instead. This lesson unpacks the principle and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal ruling
State that tahlif is a mutual oath procedure in sale disputes. Its application is limited by the principle stated.
2
Analyze the condition (shart)
The shart for tahlif is that the parties must disagree on the ma'qud 'alayhi (sold item) or ma'qud bihi (price). If the dispute is about anything else, tahlif does not apply.
This is the core definition.
3
Examine contrary examples
The passage lists disputes over payment term, khiyar (option), and receipt of part of the price. These do not trigger tahlif; instead, the denying party takes an oath alone.
Ancillary matters.
4
Recognize intra-Hanafi consensus
All early Hanafi authorities agree on this limitation, making it a relied-upon position (mufta bihi).
5
Contrast with Shafi'i position
Shafi'is apply tahlif to any dispute in the contract, not just the core elements. Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).
Highlights Hanafi specificity.

Mutual oath depends on dispute regarding the subject matter of the contract and the price. Translation: The basis of mutual oath is dispute over the ma'qud 'alayhi and ma'qud bihi. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that both parties will take oaths only when their dispute is over the ma'qud 'alayhi and ma'qud bihi, i.e., the actual sold item or price. If the dispute is not over the ma'qud 'alayhi and ma'qud bihi, then mutual oath will not be implemented. The author of Al-Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, is explaining that if the seller and buyer differ over the term (payment period), or a condition of option, or the receipt of part of the price, then mutual oath will not occur; that is, oaths will not be taken from both, but rather the one who denies will take the oath. Because in the mentioned cases, the dispute is not over the ma'qud 'alayhi and ma'qud bihi, and when there is no dispute over them, mutual oath is not initiated. Rather, mutual oath is for cases of dispute over the ma'qud 'alayhi (sold item) and ma'qud bihi (price). (And if both produce evidence, the witness to the increase is preferred because witnesses are for proof; whichever is more probative is stronger and prevails over the other. If the dispute is over both price and sold item, then the seller's evidence regarding the price is preferred because it is more probative, and the buyer's evidence regarding the sold item is preferred because it is more probative. Al-Ikhtiyar li-Ta'lil al-Mukhtar: Kitab al-Da'wa, section: Dispute between seller and buyer over price or sold item, vol. 2, p. 119) And if each of them produces evidence, the evidence proving the increase is preferred because witnesses are for proof and there is no contradiction between them regarding the increase; so whichever is more probative is preferred. (Al-'Inayah: Chapter of Tahlif, vol. 8, p. 205) Al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Da'wa, Chapter of Tahlif, vol. 3, p. 218.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage defines the scope of mutual oath (tahlif) in sale disputes. The principle states that tahlif applies only when the parties disagree over the subject matter of the contract (ma'qud 'alayhi) or the price (ma'qud bihi). Disputes over other matters—such as the term of payment, a condition of option (khiyar), or the receipt of part of the price—do not trigger mutual oath. Instead, only the denying party takes an oath. The commentator explains that this is because tahlif is designed to resolve fundamental disagreements about the essence of the contract, not ancillary conditions. The passage then quotes from Al-Ikhtiyar and Al-'Inayah revisiting the earlier principle about witnesses for increase, but this is part of the same discussion from Al-Hidayah. The key takeaway is that tahlif is a specific procedure limited to core contractual disputes, and it is not applied to incidental differences.

Key Terms
التَّحَالُفُ (al-tahāluf): Mutual oath-taking between the seller and buyer when they dispute over the subject matter or price and both lack evidence, leading to rescission of the sale.
الْمَعْقُودِ عَلَيْهِ (al-ma'qūd 'alayh): The object of the contract, i.e., the sold item (mabī').
الْمَعْقُودِ بِهِ (al-ma'qūd bih): The price (thaman) in a sale contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Whenever they disagree on any term of the sale.
B) Only when they disagree on the subject matter (mabi') or price (thaman).
C) Only when they disagree on the payment term.
D) Only when both produce witnesses.

Key Takeaway
Mutual oath in sale disputes is strictly reserved for disagreements over the sold item or price; ancillary disputes do not trigger it.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
6 min
When Mutual Oaths Apply in Sales Disputes
Rule stating that mutual oaths depend on disagreement over subject matter and price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for applying tahāluf and distinguish between core and ancillary disputes to determine when mutual oaths are required.

Core Idea
Tahāluf only applies when the dispute touches the very substance of the contract—the item or the price—not its incidentals.

Mutual oaths (tahāluf) are a powerful tool in sales disputes, leading to contract rescission. But not every disagreement triggers them. The Hanafi school strictly limits tahāluf to conflicts over the sold item or the price. This lesson unpacks that rule and contrasts it with the broader view of Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core elements of a sales contract
The ma'qud 'alayh (sold item) and ma'qud bihi (price) are essential. Disputes over these are fundamental.
These are the only triggers for tahāluf according to Hanafis.
2
Distinguish ancillary matters
Term (ajal), option (shart al-khiyar), and partial receipt of price are ancillary—they modify the contract but do not define its core.
Ancillary disputes do not trigger mutual oaths.
3
Apply the rule: check core vs. ancillary
If the dispute is about the item or price, tahāluf applies. If it is about an ancillary matter, only the denier takes an oath.
This is the mufta bihi position.
4
Compare the opposing view
Zufar and al-Shafi'i analogize the term to a description that affects price; they treat it as core, so they apply tahāluf.
They extend the analogy to option and partial receipt as well.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Hanafi: contract stands after denier's oath. Opposing: both swear, contract annulled.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

الهداية كتاب الدعوى باب التحالف، ج ۳ ص ۲۱۸
Mutual oaths depend on disagreement regarding the subject matter and the consideration. Translation: The basis of mutual oaths is disagreement about the subject matter and consideration. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that both parties are made to take oaths only when their disagreement is about the subject matter and consideration, i.e., the actual sold item or the price. If there is no disagreement about the subject matter and consideration, then mutual oaths do not apply. The author of al-Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if the seller and buyer disagree about the term, or about the condition of option, or about receipt of part of the price, then there is no mutual oath, meaning both parties do not take oaths; rather, only the denier takes the oath, because in the mentioned cases the disagreement is not about the subject matter and consideration. And when there is no disagreement about the subject matter and consideration, mutual oaths are not applied; rather, mutual oaths occur only in cases of disagreement about the subject matter (the sold item) and the consideration (the price). (And if both produce evidence, the evidence proving the increase is more deserving, because evidences are for proof, and whichever is more proof is stronger, so it prevails over the other. And if the disagreement is about both the price and the sale, then the seller's evidence regarding the price is more deserving, because it is more proven, and the buyer's evidence regarding the sold item is more deserving, because it is more proven. (al-Ikhtiyar: book of claims, chapter: disagreement of seller and buyer about price or sold item, vol. 2, p. 119) And if each of them produces evidence, the evidence that establishes the increase is more deserving, because evidences are for proof and there is no contradiction between them regarding the increase, so whichever is more proven is stronger. (al-Inayah: chapter of mutual oaths, vol. 8, p. 205) al-Hidayah, book of claims, chapter of mutual oaths, vol. 3, p. 218)
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents a rule from al-Hidayah regarding the application of mutual oaths (tahāluf) in disputes between buyer and seller. The principle states that tahāluf only applies when the disagreement pertains to the essential elements of the contract: the subject matter (ma'qud 'alayh, i.e., the sold item) and the consideration (ma'qud bihi, i.e., the price). If the dispute is about ancillary matters such as the term of payment (ajal), a condition of option (shart al-khiyar), or the receipt of part of the price, then tahāluf does not apply. Instead, only the denier of the claim is required to take an oath. The author then quotes from al-Ikhtiyar and al-Inayah to support this, emphasizing that when both parties present evidence, the evidence that establishes an increase (e.g., a higher price or additional item) is given precedence because it provides stronger proof. This is a Hanafi position; the passage implicitly contrasts with the views of Zufar and al-Shafi'i, who would apply tahāluf in such cases by analogy to disputes over the description of the price. The Hanafi reasoning is that these are not disputes over the core subject matter, so tahāluf is not triggered.

Key Terms
تحالف (tahāluf): A procedure where both parties to a contract dispute take oaths, leading to rescission of the contract.
معقود عليه (ma‘qūd ‘alayh): The subject matter of the contract, e.g., the sold item.
معقود به (ma‘qūd bihi): The consideration of the contract, e.g., the price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute between buyer and seller, when does tahāluf (mutual oaths) apply according to the Hanafi school?
A) Whenever there is any disagreement about the contract terms.
B) Only when the disagreement is about the subject matter (sold item) or the price.
C) Only when the disagreement is about the delivery time or place.
D) It never applies; only the plaintiff's oath is taken.

Key Takeaway
Mutual oaths (tahāluf) are limited to disputes over the sold item or price; for ancillary terms, only the denier swears.

The Denier of Contingencies: A Sales Dispute Rule
Rule and dispute regarding the acceptance of the denier's word over contingencies in sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish disputes over contract contingencies from those over core elements, and apply the Hanafi rule that the denier of the contingency is believed upon oath without mutual oaths.

Core Idea
Disputes about contract contingencies (ajal, khiyar) do not trigger mutual oaths; the denier of the contingency is believed on oath.

In this passage from al-Hidayah, we encounter a key procedural rule in sales disputes: when the disagreement is about an ancillary condition (a contingency) rather than the object or price, the party who denies the condition is believed upon taking an oath. This rule avoids the more severe mutual oath (tahāluf) procedure that would otherwise annul the contract. We will explore the Hanafi position, the opposing view of Zufar and al-Shafi'i, and the reasoning behind each.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the general rule: the denier of a contingency (such as a term or option condition) is believed upon oath, and no mutual oaths occur.
This applies when the dispute is about the existence or amount of a contingency, not the core sale elements.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Zufar (a Hanafi disciple) and al-Shafi'i hold that mutual oaths should be taken because the contingency is like a description of the price, which would normally trigger tahāluf.
They analogize the term to a price description since the price varies with the term.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opponent argues that since the price increases with a longer term, a dispute about the term is effectively a dispute about the price description, which necessitates mutual oaths per the earlier rule (principle 88).
This is a qiyas (analogical reasoning) from the general rule on price description disputes.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the dispute is not about the ma'qud 'alayh (subject matter) or ma'qud bihi (consideration), which are the only triggers for tahāluf; therefore, the default rule applies: the denier is believed on oath.
The contingency is separate from the essential elements.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under the Hanafi view, only one party swears and the contract stands; under the opposing view, both swear and the contract is annulled.
This difference affects the stability of contracts and the burden of proof.

Commentary
This passage presents a general rule from al-Hidayah: 'The word is of the denier of contingencies (awārid).' Contingencies here refer to ancillary conditions that may affect the contract, such as a term (ajal) or an option condition (shart al-khiyar). When a dispute arises between the seller and buyer over such a contingency, the party who denies its existence is believed upon taking an oath. The author clarifies that this is because the dispute is not about the core elements of the contract (the item and the price), so mutual oaths (tahāluf) do not apply; instead, only the denier swears. The passage then quotes al-Inayah and Durar al-Hukkam to reinforce the point, including a rebuttal of the view of Zufar and al-Shafi'i, who would apply mutual oaths by analogy to disputes over descriptions of the price. The Hanafi response is that these are not descriptions of the price but separate contingencies, and the dispute is outside the ma'qud 'alayh and ma'qud bihi. Thus, the rule is a practical application of the earlier principle (88) that tahāluf is limited to disputes over the essential contract elements.

Key Terms
عوارض (‘awārid): Ancillary conditions or contingencies in a contract, such as a term (ajal) or option condition (shart al-khiyar).
منكر العوارض (munkir al-‘awārid): The party in a dispute who denies the existence of the ancillary condition.

Key Takeaway
When a dispute arises over a contract contingency (not core elements), the Hanafi school accepts the word of the party who denies the contingency, upon oath, without mutual oaths.

The Principle of the Denier of Extraordinary Conditions
Explaining the maxim and its application in sales disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-qawl li-munkir al-cawarid' to determine whether mutual oath-swearing (tahaluf) is required in disputes about external conditions in a sale contract.

Core Idea
In disputes about non-essential conditions, the denier's sworn statement prevails — no mutual oath-swearing needed.

Buyer and seller agreements often include conditions like deferred payment or an option to cancel. But when a dispute arises about whether such a condition was agreed, how should a judge decide? This lesson explores a key Hanafi procedural maxim: the word of the party who denies the extraordinary condition is accepted with an oath. We will see how this principle resolves common sales disputes and how it differs from the views of other scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Define the specific point of disagreement: is it about the essence of the sale (object or price) or about an extra condition?
The passage lists disputes about ajal (term), shart al-khiyar (option), receipt of partial payment, and place of delivery.
2
State the Hanafi principle
Memorize the maxim 'al-qawl li-munkir al-cawarid' and its meaning: the one who denies an extraordinary condition has the saying with his oath.
This principle is a qawida sughra in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Recognize the opposing view
Identify that Zufar and al-Shafi'i analogize the term to a description (wasf) that affects price, thus requiring mutual oaths.
Their argument: ajal increases price → like a description → tahaluf.
4
Apply the Hanafi response
Understand that the Hanafi rebuttal distinguishes external conditions (cawarid) from essential elements and descriptions. Rule: no tahaluf.
Key phrase: 'ghayr al-ma'qud alayh wa al-ma'qud bihi'.
5
Note the practical consequence
In court, a Hanafi judge accepts the denier's oath; a Shafi'i judge requires mutual oaths and rescinds the contract.
The thamarah al-khilaf is the procedural outcome.

Commentary
This passage presents principle (89) from Usool al-Hidayah: "The statement is for the denier of extraordinary conditions" (al-qawl li-munkir al-cawarid). It is a ruling type passage with proof, where the author explains that in disputes between buyer and seller about conditions such as deferred payment (ajal) or option of stipulation (shart al-khiyar), the party who denies these conditions has the saying, supported by an oath. The passage then cites a longer text from al-Hidayah which clarifies that there is no tahaluf (mutual oath-swearing) in these disputes, contrary to the views of Zufar and al-Shafi'i, who argue that since the term affects the price like a description, difference in description warrants tahaluf. The Hanafi response (lana) is that this difference is not about the subject matter (al-ma'qud alayh) or the price (al-ma'qud bihi), but about external conditions, and thus does not require tahaluf. The key principle is that the denier of an extraordinary circumstance (aariḍ) is believed. This is a fundamental maxim in Hanafi fiqh for resolving disputes in sales contracts.

Key Terms
عوارض (cawarid): Extraordinary circumstances or conditions that are not essential to the contract, such as deferred payment or option of stipulation.
تحالف (tahaluf): Mutual oath-swearing between two parties in a dispute, where each takes an oath regarding their claim.
الأجل (al-ajal): The deferred term or deadline for payment in a sale.
شرط الخيار (shart al-khiyar): The condition of option in a sale, allowing one or both parties to cancel the contract within a specified period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The contract is automatically rescinded.
D) The buyer's statement is always accepted.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'al-qawl li-munkir al-cawarid' ensures that in disputes about non-essential conditions, the party denying the condition prevails with an oath, avoiding the need for mutual oath-swearing and contract rescission.

Destruction of the Sold Item Prevents Mutual Oath-Swearing
Stating that destruction of goods prevents tahaluf according to Shaykhayn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when the sold item is destroyed after delivery and explain why no mutual oath-swearing occurs.

Core Idea
When the sold item is destroyed, tahaluf is impossible; instead, the buyer's statement is accepted.

We now examine a crucial procedural rule in sales disputes: what happens when the sold item is already gone? According to the two Shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), the destruction of the item prevents mutual oath-swearing (tahaluf). This lesson unpacks the reasoning, the intra-Hanafi difference with Imam Muhammad, and the practical implications for dispute resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
The scenario: buyer takes possession of the sold item (mabi‘), then the item is destroyed. Afterwards, a dispute arises between buyer and seller regarding the amount of the price.
The destruction occurs after the buyer has taken physical possession.
2
State the Ruling of the Two Shaykhs
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, no mutual oath-swearing (tahaluf) occurs. Instead, the buyer's statement with his oath is accepted because he is the one in possession.
The buyer is the denier of any extra condition (like a higher price) and thus his word is taken.
3
Understand the Proof
The basis for tahaluf (from Hadith) is conditional on the existence of the sold item. Since the item no longer exists, the evidentiary basis for tahaluf disappears.
This is a rational deduction from the textual evidence: tahaluf is only prescribed when the item can be present for the oath process.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees: he holds that tahaluf can still occur because the oath itself remains possible. However, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view is that of the two Shaykhs.
The difference turns on whether the existence of the item is an implicit condition or merely a factual circumstance.
5
Conclude the Practical Result
In practice, when the sold item is destroyed after delivery and a dispute arises, the judge accepts the buyer's statement (with his oath) regarding the price, and no mutual oath-swearing takes place.
This protects the buyer from having to swear an oath when the item is no longer available for verification.

The author of al-Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if after the buyer takes possession of the sold item, the item is destroyed, then a dispute between seller and buyer regarding the amount of the price occurs, then according to the two Shaykhs, tahaluf will not occur, but rather the buyer's statement will be accepted, because the mentioned: His statement (And the statement is for the one who denies the option and the term with his oath) because they establish a conflict: the condition and the statement is for the denier of extraordinary conditions. And this is the meaning of his statement: (because they) i.e. because the term and the option are established by an extraordinary condition, meaning by a condition that is incidental to the original contract, and the statement is for the denier of extraordinary conditions. And this is the apparent ruling. Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents principle (90) from Usool al-Hidayah: "The destruction of the goods prevents mutual oath-swearing according to the two Shaykhs" (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). It is a ruling statement with proof. The author explains that if the sold item (mabi') is destroyed after the buyer has taken possession, and then a dispute arises between buyer and seller regarding the price amount, the two Shaykhs rule that no tahaluf occurs. This is because the proof for tahaluf from Hadith is conditional upon the existence of the sold item. Instead, the buyer's statement is accepted with his oath, as he is the one in possession. The passage then quotes from al-Jawharah al-Nirah, al-Binayah, and Fath al-Qadir to reinforce the principle that the denier of options and terms (as extraordinary conditions) has the saying. Note that here, the buyer is effectively the denier of any extra condition that would have required him to pay more, so his word is taken.

Key Terms
هلاك السلعة (halak al-sil'ah): The destruction or perishing of the sold item after its delivery to the buyer.
الشيخين (al-shaykhayn): The two Shaykhs: Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two Shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), what happens if the sold item is destroyed after the buyer takes possession and then a dispute arises about the price?
A) The seller and buyer must perform mutual oath-swearing (tahaluf).
B) The contract is void and the price must be returned.
C) No mutual oath-swearing occurs; the buyer's statement with oath is accepted.
D) The seller's statement with oath is accepted.

Key Takeaway
Destruction of the sold item after delivery prevents mutual oath-swearing; the buyer's statement with his oath is accepted.
